<FIRST>r i PM-China-Dissident     01-09 0580</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-China-Dissident,0605</SECOND>
<HEAD>Pro-Democracy Leader Released From Shanghai Jail</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By JOHN POMFRET</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Asociated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>BEIJING (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   China today released a 33-year-old dissident who
was active in the pro-democracy movement in Shanghai and whose case
was taken up by the U.S. Congress.
   Yang Wei, a former student at the University of Arizona, walked
from Ti Lan Qiao Prison in Shanghai today after serving one year of
a two-year sentence.
   Fu Fenghao, an official at the Shanghai Foreign Affairs office,
said because Yang spent about a year in custody before being
sentenced, he was let out on time.
   In a telephone interview with The Associated Press, Yang said his
first concern upon release was the health of his father, who has
cancer.
   ``I'm going to see him today,'' said Yang. ``He is in the
hospital and he is in serious condition.''
   Yang was sentenced in December 1987 for ``demagogical propoganda
for counterrevolutionary ends.''
   He had been held without charges since January 1987 in connection
with pro-democracy student demonstrations in Shanghai in 1986.
   Yang said he did not regret marching for democracy and writing
for ``China Spring,'' the banned periodical of the Chinese Alliance
for Democracy, a New York-based dissident group. But he said he was
unclear whether he would continue working for political change in
China.
   ``I just got out,'' he said, ``so I don't really understand
what's happening in society. When I determine that then I will make
a decision.''
   Yang described his treatment in prison as ``pretty good.''
   ``It seemed that I was given special treatment,'' he said.
   Yang, who received a master's degree in molecular biology from
Arizona, said he wanted to return to the United States to continue
his studies.
   Sources close to the family said Yang's release was delayed by
several hours today because three members of the Chinese Alliance
for Democracy were apprehended in Shanghai over the weekend with
copies of ``China Spring'' and a letter for Yang.
   The alliance was set up in 1983 by a former Chinese citizen to
promote democracy in China. Chinese officials have declared it an
illegal, reactionary organization.
   Two of the men _ Huang Han of Canada and Bell M.S. Wong, an
American who also goes by the name Huang Wencheng _ were ordered out
of China and flew to Hong Kong, the official China Daily said today.
   The third man, Tang Guangzhong, flew back to the United States to
continue studies at Utah State University.
   The report said the three acknowledged they were members of the
alliance. It also said they had admitted breaking the law.
   It was not clear why Tang, a Chinese citizen, was allowed to
return to the United States.
   During Yang's trial in 1987, the court said he put up
``reactionary slogans'' at Shanghai's Fudan University on Dec. 22,
1986 and sent letters to students in Canton and Beijing inciting
them to protest.
   Hundreds of thousands of students marched in dozens of cities
during the winter of 1986-87, calling for democracy.
   On Dec. 3, 1987, the U.S. Congress passed an amendment to a State
Department appropriations bill calling for Yang's release, saying he
had broken no Chinese or U.S. laws.
   China called the amendment ``an undisguised interference in
China's internal affairs.''
   Yang's wife, Che Shaoli, lives in Houston and studies
microbiology at the Baylor College of Medicine.
   She said she was thrilled at the news of his release.
   ``But I am sad, too,'' she said. ``I don't know when I will see
him.''
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r i PM-Britain-Iran     01-09 0384</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Britain-Iran,0397</SECOND>
<HEAD>Briton Freed From Iranian Jail Speaks of Experiences</HEAD>
<DATELINE>LONDON (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   A 23-year-old Briton freed after more than two
years in a Tehran jail claims he was trying to join Afghan
guerrillas when Iranian border guards seized him on the
Iran-Pakistan frontier.
   The Mail on Sunday quoted Nicholas Nicola, who arrived back in
Britain on Dec. 27 after his release, as saying he had been kept
mostly in solitary confinement except for a weekly shower and
interrogation.
   He also confirmed reports that he had been tried, although
because the trial was held in the Farsi language, said he had been
unaware he was serving a 3{-year sentence for possessing weapons,
the newspaper reported.
   Nicola bought a ticket to Pakistan in 1986 in order to join the
Afghan rebels fighting the Soviet-backed Communist government, the
Mail said.
   ``My thoughts were to go and find the mujahedeen (Moslem
guerrillas) ... so I could fight against the Russians,'' the weekly
paper quoted him as saying. Soviets troops have backed Afghanistan's
communist government against the guerrillas in a decade-old conflict.
   Dressed in Afghan clothes and carrying a rifle and pistol he had
bought at a market, Nicola told the paper he was in Pakistan on the
border with Iran when an Iranian patrol began running toward him.
   ``I think they saw my guns,'' he was quoted as saying.
   ``One fired two shots and, without thinking, I fired two shots
into the air with the rifle,'' he was quoted as saying.
   The Iranians beat Nicola, screamed at him and then took him to
prison, he said. After his trial, he remained at Tehran's maximum
security Evin Prison, the report said.
   Nicola said he wasn't sure what to do with his life now, the
paper reported.
   ``I have been too naive,'' he was quoted as saying. ``I had
romantic ideas from the movies. I know it isn't like that now.''
   His release was the latest sign of a thaw in relations between
the countries since they virtually cut diplomatic ties in 1987 after
a dispute which began when British police arrested an Iranian envoy
on shoplifting charges.
   Roger Cooper, a British businessman who has lived in Iran for
more than 20 years, has been jailed there without trial for more
than three years on charges of spying.

<SECOND>PM-Mardi Gras-Cross Dressing,0328</SECOND>
<HEAD>Mardi Gras Organza Brings Out His `Art'</HEAD>
<DATELINE>NEW ORLEANS (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   He describes himself as an extremist, a
perfectionist, a man who likes to shock people, and a man who never
wears the same dress twice.
   When he slips into a black organza evening gown, he doesn't
consider it simply cross-dressing but a craft and a challenge:
``Take it the whole distance or not at all.''
   But Gene, who asked that his last name be withheld, does not
change his voice.
   ``I am not trying to pass myself off as a woman. I want them to
know I am a man. I want them to see the art in what I am doing,'' he
said in an interview published Sunday in The Times-Picayune.
   For Gene, 32, this time of high fashion and late-night,
last-minute fittings got its first audience Sunday night at the
Municipal Auditorium, as he was the master of ceremonies at the
Krewe of Polyphemus' annual gay ball.
   On Feb. 7, Mardi Gras, the season will conclude with the Bourbon
Street Awards, which Gene has been master of ceremonies for the past
six years. Only once did he yield to suggestions that he do the show
as plain old Gene: He wore a fez and his performance suffered.
   Only once did his job as a clothing store manager require him to
miss Mardi Gras.
   ``It almost killed me,'' he said.
   Last year, drawing inspiration from Cher, he donned a black body
stocking that left only small, beaded bits of the anatomy to the
viewer's imagination.
   This year, even less will be more _ a trend that could drive him
into retirement.
   ``I feel a tremendous amount of pressure. I can keep coming up
with costumes, but I can't take off any more clothes than I've
already taken off,'' he said.
   ``How many more ways can I show skin and still be creative?''
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r a PM-Lites     01-09 0432</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Lites,0447</SECOND>
<HEAD>On The Light Side</HEAD>
<DATELINE>MILTON, Pa. (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   This normally quiet central Pennsylvania
community of about 7,700 has issued a rather loud eviction notice to
unwanted crows.
   After five days last week of trying to shoo the birds with loud
noise, officials said the number of crow sightings in town markedly
decreased.
   About 35,000 crows were estimated to be in the area at sundown
Friday as a propane gas-fired cannon was being set up to scare them.
The birds soon flew away.
   Police concluded the effort against the birds Saturday morning by
firing off several noisemakers to chase the few crows that had
returned.
   Various kinds of fireworks had been set off since Tuesday to
convince the birds that trees in Milton are unacceptable places for
them to roost at night.
   It remains to be seen whether the crows will return, so residents
have continued their noisemaking to ensure that their unwanted
visitors stay away.
   Resident Vida Switzer, for instance, bangs a wooden spoon against
a cast-iron frying pan, and Thomas Deans claps his hands when he
walks to and from work.
</TEXT>
<DATELINE>FRANKLIN, Ind. (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Byron Pursifull says he's never seen a tree
he didn't like.
   And the 61-year-old building and maintenance supervisor for the
Johnson County Parks and Recreation Department intends to leave his
mark on the county in the form of greenery.
   The founder of the Johnson County Garden Club, Pursifull has
taught schoolchildren to plant crab apple trees and has designed
landscaping and planted trees in the Johnson County Park.
   Pursifull's devotion to tree planting has earned him the
attention of the National Arbor Day Foundation, which plans to honor
him April 29.
   He is one of two people who will receive the Lawrence Enerson
Award, named after a former trustee of the Arbor Day Foundation. The
award honors those whose life work exemplifies commitment to tree
planting and conservation as part of community improvement.
   ``When he's talking, they (children) listen to him and you can
tell in their eyes how interested they are in what he has to say,''
said Don G. Wertz, science coordinator at Custer Baker Middle School
in Franklin, who nominated Pursifull for the award. ``When he talks
about trees, he refers to them as `little fellows.' He's such a nice
gentleman to have around kids.''
   Pursifull's current project: planting trees at the Johnson County
Park to honor anyone who is a county resident.
   ``I sincerely hope future generations will be able to stroll
around this site while they think of loved ones honored with a
living tree,'' he said.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>u i PM-Japan-Markets     01-09 0361</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Japan-Markets,0375</SECOND>
<HEAD>Stocks Dollar, Rise In Japan</HEAD>
<DATELINE>TOKYO (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Share prices surged on the Tokyo Stock Exchange
today in the first day of trading after Emperor Hirohito's death,
and the dollar edged higher against the Japanese yen for the third
consecutive session.
   The Nikkei Stock Average of 225 selected shares, a 25.75-point
gainer Friday, shot up 468.85 points, or 1.5 percent, closing at
30,678.39 points, a record high. The previous record was 30,243.66
last Wednesday.
   It was the largest one-day advance since Jan. 6, 1988.
   Saturday's half-day session of stock trading was canceled after
Emperor Hirohito died of cancer early in the day.
   A dealer at Nomura Securities said small investors were drawn
back into the market after the emperor's death.
   ``During his three-month-long illness, many individual investors
were extremely cautious and anxious about what might happen when the
emperor died,'' said Michio Katsumata. ``But that's over now.''
   Katsumata added that January is traditionally a bullish month for
the Tokyo Stock Exchange, and that investors also were responding to
positive economic conditions in Japan.
   Buying interest gradually spread to institutional investors as
prices kept drifting higher through the day, other traders said.
   Dealers said shares were bought on expectations of higher prices
ahead. Many economists have predicted Japan's economy will continue
to boom until the middle of this year, due partly to continuing
domestic demand.
   ``As much as it is discouraged by the death of Hirohito, the
market has high hopes for the beginning of a new era,'' said Koji
Kamemura, head of Yasuda Mutual Insurance Co.'s fund management
division.
   Kamemura said the yen's stability in Tokyo also is providing the
market with more reason for bullish sentiment.
   On the foreign exchange market, the dollar closed at 126.08 yen,
0.11 yen higher than Friday's close of 125.97 yen. It opened at
126.05 yen and traded between 125.85 yen and 126.23 yen.
   The dollar now has gained 2.03 yen in three trading sessions.
   Trading was described as restrained. Some foreign exchange
dealers had anticipated the yen would dip to as low as 129 yen to
the dollar after Hirohito's death.
   An estimated $5.5 billion were exchanged in spot trading today.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>u i PM-Afghanistan     01-09 0566</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Afghanistan,0587</SECOND>
<HEAD>Guerrillas Break Off Talks with Soviet Union</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By KATHY GANNON</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>ISLAMABAD, Pakistan (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Afghan guerrilla leaders refused today
to resume direct talks with the Soviets, accusing Moscow of playing
political games aimed at securing its communist proteges in Kabul a
place in a post-war government.
   The guerrillas promised not to attack departing Red Army troops
but said their decision to break off negotiations means fighting is
likely to continue after Feb. 15, when the 50,000 Soviet troops
remaining in Afghanistan are to withdraw under a U.N.-brokered
accord.
   The Pakistan-based guerrilla leaders ended talks with Soviet
Deputy Foreign Minsiter Yuli Vorontsov on Saturday, deadlocked over
the composition of a post-war government but saying they were ready
to meet again.
   But Vorontsov later told reporters that Moscow would find a way
to support its Marxist allies in Kabul if a broad-based government
that includes communists is not in place Feb. 15.
   Moslem insurgents, who have been fighting the Soviet-backed
communist regime in Kabul for a decade, have unanimously rejected
the participation of any of its members in a post-war government.
   Today, the spokesman for the U.S.-backed insurgents, Sibghatullah
Mojaddidi told a news conference:
   ``Because the Soviets use threats, look for war rather than
peace, and disrespect Afghans' right of self-determination, we
believe that negotiations are unproductive and unnecessary.
   ``The Soviets have threatened that if their terms are not
accepted they will supply new weapons, not deployed before, to
(Afghan President) Najib's regime after the withdrawal,'' said
Mojaddidi. ``The Soviets' efforts to impose a government of their
choice on the Afghan nation will protract the war and kill our
people.''
   Although Mojaddidi ruled out the cease-fire sought by Vorontsov
but promised that Moslem guerrillas, called mujahedeen or Islamic
holy warriors, would not attack departing Soviet soldiers.
   He said Vorontsov was told ``we can only order mujahedeen not to
attack your withdrawing troops. We can give you peace . . . to
pass.''
   The Soviet envoy has said Moscow will meet the February
withdrawal deadline unless departing Soviet soldiers are attacked by
guerrillas.
   ``It could be delayed if there were all out fighting on Soviet
forces, if there were attacks on Soviet forces withdrawing,''
Vorontsov said earlier.
   More than 100,000 Red Army troops moved into Afghanistan in
December 1979 and replaced one pro-Moscow regime with another. In
April, the Kremlin agreed to withdraw its forces by Feb.. Half had
left by August.
   Vorontsov has been on a diplomatic mission since early December,
seeking a political consensus on a broad-based government to step
into the breach following the withdrawal.
   He has met with the Iran- and Pakistan-based guerrilla groups,
which both agree that Najib's ruling party can play no part in a
post-war government. About 3 million Afghan refugees live in
Pakistan, another 2 million in Iran.
   Modaddidi said the decision to break off the talks means fighting
is likely to continue beyond Feb. 15.
   He predicted that the guerrillas' allies, most notably the United
States and Pakistan, will continue their support after the Red Army
leaves and predicted that the current Kabul government will collapse
within months of the final Soviet pullout.
   The Pakistan-based guerrilla alliance, the dominant insurgent
force, has said it plans to form a council that would, at the
collapse of Najib's government, choose a transitional government
from within its ranks.
   Both Pakistan- and Iran-based guerrillas would nominate council
members.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r a PM-Tornadoes     01-09 0557</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Tornadoes,0580</SECOND>
<HEAD>Tornado Cleanup Under Way In Three States</HEAD>
<HEAD>LaserPhoto NY4, VIN3</HEAD>
<DATELINE>ALLENDALE, Ill. (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Residents of three states today struggled
to recover from tornadoes that destroyed a third of the buildings in
this southeastern Illinois town and injured more than four dozen
people.
   ``It looks like this place has been bombed,'' Mayor Jack Loeffler
said Sunday from an emergency command post in a bank.
   Twisters hit the Illinois towns of Mill Shoals, Albion and
Allendale on Saturday afternoon before moving into Indiana and
touching down near Vincennes. Late Saturday, a tornado cut a
two-mile path through the southern Kentucky community of Franklin.
   Fifty people were injured in Illinois, and 22 remained
hospitalized Sunday. The three people injured in Indiana were
released from hospitals by Sunday; no injuries were reported in
Kentucky.
   ``After seeing everything that's happened, we were pretty lucky
no folks were hurt,'' said Simpson County Judge-Executive Ken Harper
in Kentucky.
   The National Guard was sent to the tornado-ravaged areas of
Illinois and Kentucky to help with the cleanup and to prevent
looting.
   Loeffler said the tornado cut through a 10-square-block area of
Allendale, destroying 53 homes and nine businesses, as well as the
post office, fire station, city hall and a school.
   On Sunday, the National Guard and other emergency workers cleared
trees and power lines that had been knocked down and other rubble
from streets, but hadn't made much headway on houses, said Gen.
Donald Lynn.
   Police said people whose homes were destroyed were staying with
friends or relatives in town, and about a dozen were taken to
shelters.
   Shocked officials and residents of this town of 600 assessed the
damage Sunday.
   ``It's a mess is all you can say,'' said Allendale resident Linda
Griesemer. ``Houses are flattened, windows blowed out, roofs gone.''
   A damage estimate was not yet available, Loeffler said.
   Gov. James R. Thompson toured Allendale and flew over the other
affected areas. He said he planned to declare Allendale a state
disaster area.
   ``It's a mess. It's awful,'' Thompson said. The tornado ``not
only took out a third of the homes, it took out almost the entire
downtown and all the public facilities.''
   In Mill Shoals, two or three mobile homes were overturned and a
few homes and farm buildings were damaged. In Albion, five buildings
were damaged but there were no injuries, said Mayor Earl Bailey.
   In Indiana, a twister came within a mile of Vincennes Lincoln
High School, where 2,500 people were watching a junior varsity
basketball game when the storm knocked the school's power out.
   ``People are moving back into their homes and salvaging what they
can,'' Sheriff's Deputy Jerry Reitmeyer said Sunday. ``We were lucky
last night. It didn't rain anymore after the tornado, so not a lot
of homes were water-soaked.
   ``One or two homes are really damaged extensively enough that the
people won't be able to live in them.''
   Five homes were destroyed and 25 were damaged by the tornado that
hit Franklin, Ky.
   ``It came and went so quickly that some people slept through it,
didn't know it had happened,'' Harper said.
   William Huff said he went to his front door to investigate the
booming sounds of the tornado.
   ``I opened the front door, and the next thing I know, the house
was gone,'' Huff said. His two-bedroom house was leveled.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r a PM-ConfederateSpy     01-09 0580</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Confederate Spy,0597</SECOND>
<HEAD>Young Confederate Spy Remembered On Anniversary Of Execution</HEAD>
<HEAD>Eds: Capitalization in 16th graf is cq</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By SCOTT CHARTON</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>LITTLE ROCK, Ark. (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   David O. Dodd took his secrets to the
grave after he was hanged at age 17 for being a Confederate spy 125
years ago.
   A coded message detailing Union positions around Little Rock
after the city fell to Union troops in September 1863 was found in
his boot.
   But Dodd refused to answer questions or testify at a
court-martial.
   Dodd was convicted of spying for the Confederacy and hanged Jan.
8, 1864.
   On Sunday, Civil War enthusiasts gathered at Dodd's plot in Mount
Holly Cemetery to mark the 125th anniversary of his execution. About
40 people _ half in Civil War dress _ stood in the cold as William
W. O'Donnell of Little Rock, author of articles about Dodd,
proclaimed him the ``Boy Martyr of the Confederacy.''
   ``It's a story of unbounded courage,'' O'Donnell said.
   A crowd of 6,000, some of whom walked across the frozen Arkansas
River, witnessed Dodd's hanging near downtown Little Rock. The
execution took five minutes, and wasn't over until a Union soldier
climbed the gallows to jerk the rope, O'Donnell said.
   ``Dodd was one of the youngest ever executed as a spy,'' said
Jerry Russell, a member of the Civil War Roundtable of Arkansas.
   Dodd's fate began to unravel in late December 1863. He traveled
south from Little Rock, surrendering his pass to get through a Union
roadblock. After spending the night with a relative, Dodd headed
back toward the occupied city.
   ``The Union lines had moved out a couple of miles the next day.
Since Dodd had surrendered his pass, he couldn't get through,''
Russell said.
   ``There wasn't a real tight ring of steel around the town. He
might have gotten through with his information. If Dodd had really
been the sneaky type, he would have gone through the woods.
Apparently, he was not sneakily inclined, and thought he could go
through the roadblock.''
   The offer to betray his allies was extended to Dodd by Gen.
Frederick Steele, who, ``in his written reports, felt really bad
about it. He was very anguished over this thing, but they could not
condone spying,'' Russell said.
   Dodd also wouldn't tell why he was carrying the message, which
was in Morse code. ``Why did he do it? Why? He never told anybody,''
O'Donnell said. ``He never took the stand in his own defense. How
could a 17-year-old have so much courage to face the gallows without
trying to escape?''
   In a letter to his family on the day he died, Dodd wrote, ``I was
arrested as a Spy and tried, and Sentenced to be hung today at 3
o'clock. The time is fast approaching but thank God I am prepared to
die. I expect to meet you all in heaven. Do not weep for me for I
will be better off in heaven.''
   On Sunday, the Lord's Prayer and a 21-gun salute echoed in the
windswept cemetery. Seconds passed between each of the three volleys
as seven riflemen reloaded their black-powder weapons.
   Anthony Rushing, 26, of West Memphis, who wore a gray Civil War
coat in the ceremony's color guard, said his image of Dodd comes as
much from the heart as from the history books.
   ``He was done an injustice. I think they were trying to make an
example of him. He was a hero,'' Rushing said.
</TEXT>
</DOC>

<HEAD>NBC Chief Vows To Broadcast No More of Geraldo's Devilish Shows</HEAD>
<DATELINE>LOS ANGELES (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   It was confession time for NBC Entertainment

   Yes, Tartikoff confessed, it probably was a bad idea to broadcast

Rivera's material would be so graphic.

love _ skinning babies,''' Tartikoff said.
   NBC President and Chief Executive Robert Wright, however, said he
remains unrepentant about the Satanism special.
   Wright echoed Rivera's own defense of the program, calling the
topic ``exciting and newsworthy.''

<FILEID>AP-NR-01-09-89 0503EST</FILEID>
<FIRST>r i PM-Japan-Emperor     01-09 0648</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Japan-Emperor,0668</SECOND>
<HEAD>New Emperor Vows To Work For Peace</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By TERRIL JONES</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>TOKYO (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Emperor Akihito today set the tone for his reign by
pledging to work for peace and preserve Japanese democracy, but said
he faced ``awesome responsibilities'' after the death of his father,
Hirohito.
   The four-minute speech was Akihito's first public statement since
he ascended the Chrysanthemum Throne when Hirohito died of cancer
Saturday at age 87. It was delivered at the Imperial Palace during a
simple ceremony attended by Prime Minister Noboru Takeshita and 237
other dignitaries.
   Outside, hundreds of thousand of Japanese streamed past to pay
their respects to Hirohito, who reigned for 62 years.
   ``Shrouded in sorrow and overwhelmed by the awesome
responsibilities I undertake, I stand humble before you,'' said
Akihito, who was clad in a black cutaway and gray trousers.
   ``I pledge myself to continue in the spirit of his late majesty,
making his desire to constantly be at one with the people my desire
as well, joining together with you to preserve the constitution of
Japan, and thereby execute my duties faithfully,'' he added.
   Japan's post-war constitution, which bans the emperor from any
role in government and limits him to ceremonial functions, formed
the basis for the country's democracy. It limits the emperor's role
to signing bills into law and serving as ``symbol of the state and
the unity of the people.''
   ``I do fervently desire the further development of Japan, peace
on Earth and enhanced well-being for all peoples,'' Akihito said.
   He spoke clearly and solemnly, with the new Empress Michiko, 54,
on his left wearing a black dress and veil and their son, Crown
Prince Naruhito, 28, on his right.
   By Japanese tradition, Akihito's statement was his first Imperial
Rescript, in which a new emperor sets a tone for his reign.
   The rite, in the palace's main ceremonial hall, was an important
milestone in the transition to Akihito's new imperial era of Heisei,
or ``Achieving Peace.''
   On Sunday, Hirohito's body was wrapped in a white cloth and
placed in a coffin made of Japanese cypress at the palace in
preparation for the Feb. 24 burial. Representatives of more than 130
countries, including up to 50 heads of state or government, are
expected to attend.
   Hirohito's era, called Showa, or ``Enlightened Peace,'' ended
with his death, and according to Japanese tradition he becomes known
as Emperor Showa. His reign stretched from Japan's descent into
militarism and defeat in World War II to its impressive recovery
into an economic superpower.
   Thousands of police were deployed today to prevent violence by
radical leftist and rightist groups, for whom the emperor embodies
Japanese militarism and nationalism.
   Hirohito was revered as a living god until he was forced to
renounce his divinity at the end of World War II, and Japanese
soldiers fought and died during the war in the monarch's name.
   Two old soldiers have hanged themselves since learning of
Hirohito's passing, saying in suicide notes that they wanted to
follow the emperor in death. An 87-year-old former army man hanged
himself Saturday, and the body of a former navy officer was found
Sunday hanging from a tree in a temple compound.
   Mourners continued to stream to the palace today to sign
condolence registers outside the main gate. On Sunday, the palace
said 438,376 people braved drizzling rain to honor the late emperor
at the palace, and the number of signers today had reached 223,020
by 3:30 p.m.
   Japan's stock market today boomed to a record high closing and
its biggest gain in a year in the first session since Hirohito's
death.
   A dealer at Nomura Securities said small investors were drawn
back into the market after the emperor's death.
   ``During his three-month-long illness, many individual investors
were extremely cautious and anxious about what might happen when the
emperor died,'' said Michio Katsumata. ``But that's over now.''
</TEXT>

<HEAD>National Society Honors `Unbearable Lightness'</HEAD>
<DATELINE>NEW YORK (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   ``The Unbearable Lightness of Being,'' a film
adapted from Milan Kundera's novel, was voted the 1988's best
picture by the National Society of Film Critics.
   The film, about a Czech surgeon who flees his homeland when the
Soviets invade in 1968, mixes politics and eroticism. Its director,

director award.

   Actress: Judy Davis, ``High Tide.''
   Supporting Actor: Dean Stockwell, ``Married to the Mob'' and
``Tucker.''

   Documentary: ``The Thin Blue Line,'' by Errol Morris.
   Screenplay: Ron Shelton, ``Bull Durham.''
   Cinematography: Henri Alekan, ``Wings of Desire.''
   The society voted a special award to Pedro Almodovar, director of
``Women on the Verge of a Nervous Breakdown'' and ``Matador,'' ``for
his originality.''
   The society, made up of 39 critics from major publications, has
given awards for 23 years. It held its annual meeting Sunday at the
Algonquin Hotel.

<FIRST>r a PM-SkullFound     01-09 0220</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Skull Found,0228</SECOND>
<HEAD>Police Believe Skull Found By Dog Was A Homicide Victim</HEAD>
<DATELINE>TACOMA, Wash. (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   A man says he didn't pay much attention to
the bones his dog had been bringing home for the past two months
until the animal retrieved a human skull.
   The dog brought home the skull Saturday afternoon, and
authorities are treating the case as a homicide, said police
spokesman Mark Mann.
   Mann said efforts to recover the body and find clues were
hampered by snow, rain, low temperatures, and damage to the
scattered remains by dogs.
   The bones found near a city water pumphouse in northeast Tacoma
Saturday and Sunday have limited value in solving the case, police
said.
   But the skull had lots of dental work, which may help in
identifying the victim.
   Mary DeTracy, medical investigator with the Pierce County Medical
Examiner's Office, said the victim could be identified by Wednesday,
after the teeth are compared with dental records of missing persons
filed with the state patrol.
   Jim Farrell said his dog, Duke, began bringing home bones about
two months ago.
   He said he tossed most of bones in a nearby gully until Saturday
afternoon, when Duke brought home the skull.
   Most of the bones were found Sunday morning behind the pumphouse,
about 100 yards from Farrell's home, Mann said.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r a PM-42ndStreet     01-09 0405</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-42nd Street,0418</SECOND>
<HEAD>Broadway's ``42nd Street'' Closes After Eight Years</HEAD>
<DATELINE>NEW YORK (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The dancing feet of the cast of ``42nd Street''
took to the stage one last time, as the second longest-running show
on Broadway closed after eight years and 3,486 performances seen by
10 million people.
   Based on the 1933 Warner Bros. movie starring Ruby Keeler, Dick
Powell and Ginger Rogers, the show told the tale of a chorus girl
replacing an aging star who breaks her ankle just before opening
night.
   It closed on Sunday but it may be revived in April with an
all-black cast, and the scenery and other equipment is being kept.
   Its music includes such classics as ``Lullaby of Broadway,''
``Shuffle Off to Buffalo,'' and the title song.
   The musical's longevity was second only to ``A Chorus Line,''
which opened in 1975.
   ``42nd Street'' won the 1981 Tony Award for best musical, and its
director and choreographer, Gower Champion, won a posthumous Tony
for choreography.
   Champion died just hours before the opening-night performance in
New York on Aug. 25, 1980. He was 59 and had a rare form of blood
cancer.
   Few involved in putting on the musical had known of Champion's
illness, and the cast and audience were not informed of his death
until after the final curtain, when producer David Merrick announced
it from the stage.
   Merrick, now 77, suffered a stroke six years ago that makes it
difficult for him to speak.
   ``It's been a wonderful opportunity to have brought this
extravaganza to Broadway and I take pride in the fact that it has
been seen by 10 million people,'' Merrick said through his attorney.
   Merrick watched the final show from the audience and he posted a
letter backstage to thank the cast and crews for ``your brilliant
talents, your dedication, your boundless energies and caring, and
your total professionalism.''
   He has produced more than 80 Broadway shows, including ``Hello,
Dolly'' and ``Play It Again, Sam.''
   ``42nd Street'' opened with a cast of 54 that included Tammy
Grimes, Jerry Orbach, Wanda Richert and Lee Roy Reams. It closed
Sunday starring Elizabeth Allen, Jamie Ross, Jim Walton and Clare
Leach.
   Thirteen cast members had been in the show since opening night.
There have been six marriages within the company and six babies born.
   The musical played at three theaters: the Winter Garden, the
Majestic and the St. James, where its final performance was played.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r a PM-Britain-Crash-Jet     01-09 0454</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Britain-Crash-Jet,0466</SECOND>
<HEAD>Boeing Jet That Crashed Was New 737-400</HEAD>
<HEAD>With PM-Britain-Crash, Bjt</HEAD>
<DATELINE>SEATTLE (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The 12-week-old jetliner that crashed in England
was the first involving a Boeing 737-400, a model introduced last
year, a Boeing Co. spokesman said.
   The British Midland Airways jet, carrying 126 people, crashed
Sunday while attempting an emergency landing about 10 minutes after
leaving London's Heathrow Airport en routeto Belfast. Authorities
said at least 37 people were killed.
   Boeing spokesman Jack Gamble said the aircraft was purchased last
fall by GPA Leasing in Shannon, Ireland, which leased it to British
Midland. He said it is the first accident involving a 737-400 or a
737-300, which is 10 feet shorter than the 400 series.
   The 737-400 was certified by the Federal Aviation Administration
in September and 17 of the $28 million to $32 million jets are in
use, Gamble said Sunday.
   British Midland chairman Michael Bishop said the company had
taken delivery of the twin-engine plane only 12 weeks ago. He said
the plane reported severe vibrations in one of the engines about
eight minutes into the flight. Witnesses said one of the engines was
on fire as it attempted to land at an airport about 100 miles north
of London.
   Gamble said the engine used on both 737 models is the CFM-56,
which is built in France by a consortium made up of General Electric
and SMECMA, a French concern.
   He said the engines are brought to the United States and
reassembled and tested at a General Electric plant near Cincinnati.
The completed engines are shipped to the Boeing plant at Renton, a
suburb of Seattle, where the 737 is manufactured.
   The CFM-56 has been the world's top-selling commercial jet engine
during the past two years surpassing GE's rival, Pratt &amp; Whitney
Aircraft, in the market for powering standard-body jets of
short-to-medium-range routes.
   More than 5,900 of the engines have been sold to more than 100
operators since the engine entered service in 1982.
   Dwight E. Weber, a spokesman for CFM International and the GE
aircraft engine business group, declined to speculate on the role
the CFM-56 may have played in Sunday's crash.
   ``We'll be working with the airline to determine the cause,''
Weber said Sunday. ``Once the cause is determined, if it's
engine-related, we'll be working with the customers who operate the
engine to determine if it's an isolated case or a fleetwide
program.''
   A Boeing team was to travel to England today aboard a 737-300
that was being delivered to British Midland, said David Jimenez,
another Boeing spokesman.
   The 737-400 was rolled out in January 1988, and the first flight
was in March. Depending on the interior design, the jet can seat 130
to 150 passengers.
</TEXT>
</DOC>

<HEAD>Eds: UPDATES thruout with new details on wounded, number of people

<DATELINE>MANILA, Philippines (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Assailants hurled two grenades into a
crowd attending award ceremonies at a basketball tournament, killing
at least 13 people and injuring 78 in the southern Philippines, the
military said today.
   Lt. Anselmo Cabingan, a military spokesman, said authorities had
no indication of who was responsible for Sunday night's attack at a
basketball court in Esperanza, a remote town about 600 miles south
of Manila.
   About 1,000 people, many of them children, were attending the
tournament when the attack ocurred, said Sgt. Joven Astrero of the
operations section of the Philippine Constabulary in Cotabato City,
about 40 miles from Esperanza, which is in Sultan Kudarat province.
   Astrero said the tournament had just ended and the town's mayor,
Romulo Latog Sr., was awarding trophies when the blasts occurred.
Latog and his son, Romulo Jr., were among the injured, he said.
   The government-run Philippines News Agency agency, quoting a
local radio station, put the death toll at 26 people, mostly
children.
   Astrero said there were conflicting reports on the casualty
figure but it appeared the agency count was too high. Astrero could
not confirm how many children were killed.

<FIRST>r i PM-Korea-Anti-US     01-09 0290</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Korea-Anti-US,0297</SECOND>
<HEAD>Protesters Firebomb U.S. Military Base</HEAD>
<DATELINE>SEOUL, South Korea (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   A U.S. soldier was slightly injured
today when a group of protesters hurled firebombs at an American
military base in the southern city of Taegu, police and U.S.
authorities said.
   A U.S. military spokesman said the attackers hurled seven
firebombs at Camp Henry in the city 180 miles south of Seoul.
   The American soldier was hurt when a firebomb was hurled through
the windshield of the jeep he was driving, the spokesman said. The
firebomb did not explode, but the soldier was cut by flying glass,
he said.
   U.S. authorities said they did not know why the base was
attacked, but the camp and other American bases in South Korea have
been attacked in recent months by radical students demanding the
withdrawal of U.S. troops.
   Police said today's attack involved five or six youths who
identified themselves as ``patriotic students in Taegu'' in the
leaflets they scattered. Police reported no arrest.
   Police said the leaflets contained criticism of last Wednesday's
downing by U.S. warplanes of two Libyan fighters over the
Mediterranean Sea.
   It was the second attack on Camp Henry in a month. Radical
students chanting anti-U.S. slognas firebombed the camp on Dec. 27,
cauing a small fire at a building. The fire was quickly extinguished
with no injuries.
   Dissidents and radical students have staged anti-American
protests demanding the withdrawl of some 42,000 U.S. troops
stationed in South Korea under a mutual defense pact against
communist North Korea. They contend U.S. forces in the country help
perpetuate division of the Korean peninsula.
   Korea was split into the communist north and the capitalist south
in 1945, when U.S. and Soviet troops ended Japan's colonial rule
over the peninsula.
</TEXT>

<SECOND>PM-Weatherpage Weather,0628</SECOND>
<HEAD>Winter Weather Gives South A Chill; Michigan Bridge Closed</HEAD>
<HEAD>By The Associated Press</HEAD>
<TEXT>
   Moist Gulf of Mexico air in tandem with cold air produced a
potpourri of sleet, freezing rain and snow today from the lower
Mississippi Valley across the Tennessee Valley to the central
Appalachians.
   In Michigan, snow combined with winds up to 60 mph Sunday to
reduce visibility on the Mackinac Bridge and forced the Mackinac
Bridge Authority to close the structure to traffic for an hour,
authority spokeswoman Lorraine Garries said.
   ``We didn't get much accumulation, it was just blowing,'' Garries
said, adding it was only the third time in 11 years that the bridge
was forced to shut down because of weather.
   Snowfall totals across Michigan's upper peninsula ranged up to 13
inches, but the lower peninsula had snowfall totaling 1 to 4 inches.
   Wet snow fell in parts of Arkansas, Tennessee, Mississippi and
Alabama.
   It also settled over the central and southern Appalachians where
2 to 3 inches of snow accumulated. Up to 6 inches of snow was
expected in southern West Virginia, with 8 inches over higher
elevations.
   Winter weather also affected the Northwest today, with snow
falling over eastern parts of the Pacific Northwest, Montana and
Idaho. Between 6 and 12 inches of new snow was expected in parts of
Idaho by tonight.
   Meanwhile, rain fell along the central Pacific coast.
   Strong winds were blowing across the Rockies' east slopes in
Wyoming and Montana. Winds gusted to 60 mph in the upper Yellowstone
River Valley in Montana.
   Much of the nation's midsection was in the grip of a frigid
arctic air mass, with temperatures plummeting below the zero mark in
much of the northern Plains and upper Mississippi Valley.
   The frigid air was flowing eastward across the warmer waters of
the lower Great Lakes, creating lake effect snow east of Lake
Ontario and Lake Erie.
   The high Sunday was 86 degrees at Fort Myers, Fla.
   Today's forecast called for rain along much of the Pacific coast
and extending from the western and central Gulf Coast to Tennessee
and North Carolina; snow across the northern Plateau into the
northern Rocky Mountains, and in the mid-Atlantic Coast and the
southern Appalachians; scattered snow showers in the Great Lakes
region; fair to partly sunny skies across the rest of the nation.
   Predicted highs: below freezing from the northern and central
Rockies through the lower and middle Mississippi Valley to New
England; single digits and teens from eastern Montana through
Minnesota and Iowa to the upper Great Lakes; 50 degrees-plus from
the southern and central coast of California through the Rio Grande
Valley of Texas to the southern Atlantic coast, with lower 60s in
Southern California, and 70s and lower 80s in Florida.
   The nation's temperatures at 2 a.m. EST ranged from 27 degrees
below zero at Devils Lake, N.D., to 73 at Miami and Fort Lauderdale,
Fla.
   Other reports:
   _East: Atlanta 44 cloudy; Boston 38 cloudy; Buffalo 21 snow;
Charleston, S.C. 59 fair; Cincinnati 28 cloudy; Cleveland 22 snow;
Detroit 16 snow; Miami 69 fair; New York 40 cloudy; Pittsburgh 29
cloudy; Portland, Maine 35 windy; Washington 42 cloudy.
   _Central: Bismarck -16 fair; Chicago 9 partly cloudy; Dallas-Fort
Worth 35 cloudy; Denver 24 fair; Des Moines 04 fair; Indianapolis 23
cloudy; Kansas City 15 fair; Minneapolis-St. Paul -10 fair;
Nashville 35 snow; New Orleans 53 cloudy; St. Louis 23 cloudy.
   _West: Albuquerque 15 fair; Anchorage 22 partly cloudy; Las Vegas
30 fair; Los Angeles 44 fair; Phoenix 36 fair; Salt Lake City 24
cloudy; San Diego 42 fair; San Francisco 47 drizzle; Seattle 47
cloudy.
   _Canada: Montreal 23 windy; Toronto 18 snow.

<HEAD>Dollar Rises, Gold Falls</HEAD>
<DATELINE>LONDON (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The dollar rose in Europe this morning, supported
by expectations of higher U.S. interest rates. Gold prices fell.
   Traders said expectations that U.S. interest rates were poised to
rise were fueling the dollar's recent rally, but fears that central
banks may intervene were keeping a lid on the U.S. currency, at
least for the time being.
   Demand was generated mostly by speculative institutional
investors, while corporate clients appeared reluctant to commit
themselves to the dollar's recent advance, traders said.
   In London, the British pound fell to $1.7670, compared with
$1.7797 Friday.
   Other dollar rates at midmorning, compared with late Friday:
   _1.8265 West German marks, up from 1.8140
   _1.5575 Swiss francs, up from 1.5475
   _6.2265 French francs, up from 6.1830
   _2.0595 Dutch guilders, up from 2.0480
   _1,339.00 Italian lire, up from 1,330.50
   _1.1965 Canadian dollars, up from 1.1912
   In Tokyo, where trading ends before Europe's business day begins,
the dollar rose 0.11 yen to a closing 126.08 yen. Later, in London,
it was quoted at 126.55 yen.
   Gold opened in London at a bid price of $406.40 a troy ounce, up
from $405.75 bid late Friday. But at midmorning today, the city's
five major bullion dealers fixed a lower recommended price of
$404.45.
   In Zurich, the bid price was $404.80, down from $405.50 bid late
Friday.
   Earlier, in Hong Kong, gold fell $1.97 to close at a bid $407.18.
   Silver was quoted in London at a bid price of $5.97 a troy ounce,
unchanged from Friday's late price.

<HEAD>Chemical Conference Delegates Walk Out On South African Official</HEAD>
<HEAD>With PM-Chemical Conference, Bjt</HEAD>
<DATELINE>PARIS (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Foreign Minister R.F. Botha of South Africa rose to
give a speech today at an international conference on chemical
weapons and most delegates walked out in protest of his country's
apartheid policies.
   Botha then proposed an African conference on poverty.
   ``Chemical weapons are one way to die. Thirst and hunger is
another,'' he told the delegates who remained in the nearly empty
hall. They included the U.S. contingent.
   Before the speech, Foreign Minister Robert Ouko of Kenya rose and
declared:
   ``The regime in this hall does not represent the people of South
Africa. Africa will leave the hall when the clique rises to speak.''
   Most all African delegations and numerous other Soviet bloc,
Asian and Latin American delegates then cleared the hall. Only 20 of
the 150 delegations remained.
   In addition to an African meeting on poverty, Botha proposed a
conference to declare Africa a chemical weapon-free zone and to
resist Western efforts to dump toxic wastes on the continent.
   Under apartheid, South Africa's 26 million blacks have no say in
national affairs and have separate schools, neighborhoods and
medical facilities. The government is controlled by the country's 5
million whites.

<SECOND>PM-Super Bowl Tickets, 1st Ld-Writethru, a0499,0515</SECOND>
<HEAD>Fans Scrambling For Super Bowl Tickets</HEAD>
<HEAD>Eds: Leads with five grafs to ADD seating capacity and description
of how Super Bowl tickets are distributed. Picks up 5th graf, `That makes...'.</HEAD>
<DATELINE>MIAMI (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   With the Super Bowl less than two weeks away,
brokers are busy selling ticket packages for as much as $20,000 for
the football extravaganza.
   That's the going price for an air-conditioned skybox seating nine
at the 75,000-seat Joe Robbie Stadium in Miami where the Super Bowl
will be played Jan. 22.
   ``It's like an apartment,'' said Raquel Barouh, who is fielding
inquiries for the $20,000 skybox offering. ``It's worth the price.''
   The National Football League doles out Super Bowl tickets to the
league's 28 teams, with the most going to the competing teams and
the host city. The teams decide how to distribute their allotment.
   The NFL forbids the sale of tickets for a premium, and scalping
is illegal in Florida, noted James Steeg, NFL special events
coordinator.
   That makes individual ticket sellers reluctant to talk about
their offerings, but brokers who package the tickets with hotel
stays, buffets and transportation aren't so shy.
   ``We're offering $500 a seat,'' said Jerry Adelman of Murray's
Tickets in Los Angeles, where there are no restrictions on ticket
resales. ``We'll probably handle up to 3,500 seats.''
   The agency is offering a three-day package for $1,025, but
Adelman says, ``I'd rather go to Hawaii myself.''
   The Cincinnati Bengals and the San Francisco 49ers won decisive
conference championships Sunday to advance to the Super Bowl and a
shot at the NFL title.
   The Bengals, who lost the Super Bowl in 1982 to the 49ers,
defeated Buffalo 21-10 to win the American Football Conference. The
49ers beat the Chicago Bears 28-3 for the National Football
Conference title.
   Larry Reader, a salesman who lives near Indianapolis, bought 10
tickets for customers, paying $500 for six seats in the end zone and
$850 apiece for four seats on the 25-yard line.
   Wendy Lester of Miami wrote in a classified ad appeal: ``Help my
brother and I get to our first Super Bowl.''
   She ended up spending $1,000 on two tickets.
   ``It seems the things you want to do get more expensive as you
get older,'' she said.
   For $15,000, a dentist is offering four end-zone Super Bowl
tickets, a week's stay at a country club condominium and a Cadillac
for transportation.
   Dr. Ron Marx of Hollywood hasn't gotten any takers yet on his
classified ad, but he's been getting plenty of inquiries.
   ``It's unbelievable,'' he said. ``These people will pay anything.
I've been getting calls from all over the country.''
   Marx is even willing to kick in a little extra.
   ``If people really push, I might even pick them up at the
airport,'' he said, adding he has received at least a dozen phone
calls and already has the proceeds spent.
   ``I figure I can put my kid through one year of Brown University
on this,'' Marx said.
</TEXT>

<HEAD>Child Care, Abortion Issues Concern Women's Conference</HEAD>
<DATELINE>KANSAS CITY, Mo. (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The neglect of today's children could
lead to deficiencies in tomorrow's work force, Lt. Gov. Marlene
Johnson of Minnesota warned participants in the second Women's
Agenda Conference.
   In the concluding session of the conference Sunday, participants
also were asked for a renewed commitment to maintaining legalized
abortion and the development of a national coalition.
   Johnson said a lack of adequate child care kept many parents from
participating in the economy.
   Ms. Johnson also said there was a need for women in leadership
positions and called on her listeners to work to develop leaders
from within their ranks.
   ``We need to keep fighting the battles,'' she said. ``But we also
need to keep nurturing each other to become stronger and more
effective in the larger community.''
   The conference, organized by the National Federation of Business
and Professional Women's Clubs, began Friday. It was sponsored by
more than 50 organizations nationwide.
   Dolly Adams of the Black Women's Agenda encouraged the
development of a national women's coalition. She said such a group
would need to have a larger vision than those of the individual
organizations involved.
   Other issues discussed Sunday included poverty among older women,
the need to bring divorced and widowed women into the work place,
and the possibility of a renewed campaign for an Equal Rights
Amendment.
   Kate Michelman, executive director of the National Abortion
Rights Action League, said that if abortion rights are abridged,
``1989 could be the year that women were put back in their place.''
   ``We are in danger right now of losing our vital right to decide
whether to have a child or not and under what circumstances,'' she
said.
   Ms. Michelman told the audience that the 1973 Roe vs. Wade
decision by the Supreme Court occurred against the backdrop of the
women's movement.
   ``We instructed the court,'' she said. ``We became their
conscience. We created the context for Roe to happen.''
   Ms. Michelman urged the women to speak out and create a renewed
climate of activism for the pro-abortion movement.
   ``You have to go out of here absolutely committed,'' she said.
``I plead with you to be a part of millions of voices, silent no
longer.''

<SECOND>PM-Brite-Demolition Boy,0272</SECOND>
<HEAD>4-Year-Old Gets To Do Real Demolition Job</HEAD>
<DATELINE>BALTIMORE (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Kids can sure make a mess. But admittedly,
4-year-old Ian Loizeaux made a bigger mess than most.
   One push of a button by the preschooler Sunday brought an
eight-story building in downtown Baltimore crashing down to ruin.
   Doug Loizeaux, 38, says he just wanted his son to try his hand at
the family business.
   Loizeaux is co-owner of Controlled Demolition Inc. The founder of
the business, his father Jack Loizeaux, also was on hand to see the
havoc wreaked by his grandson.
   The button that Ian had pushed set off 347 pounds of explosives
that had been carefully placed in 560 locations throughout the
building so that the building would topple safely.
   ``Absolutely perfect! I'm so proud of you. Everything moved just
right,'' said the elder Loizeaux, 73.
   The boy watched in silence as the Falconer Building, which housed
printing businesses for seven decades, vanished in an avalanche of
dust. Its place will be taken by an 11-story parking garage.
   Ian's uncle, J. Mark Loizeaux, president and co-owner of the
company, said he got his own start demolishing buildings at a young
age.
   ``I started at 8,'' he said. ``That was the only time we ever saw
our dad, by going to work with him.'' He and his brother Doug, who
have six children between them ranging, say they hope to see a third
generation take over the business.
   The work is ``instant gratification,'' Doug Loizeaux said. ``You
know if you're a hero or a bum in about eight seconds.''
</TEXT>

<TEXT>
   A British investigator offered ``a
wholehearted apology'' for sending the wrong body to a family whose
daughter died aboard the Pan Am jet crash in Lockerbie, Scotland,
the young woman's father says.
   Robert Hunt said he met with a Scotland Yard official Sunday
afternoon at the Hunts' home in the Rochester suburb of Webster. His
20-year-old daughter, Karen, was among the 259 people killed aboard
Flight 103 when it exploded over Lockerbie last month.
   The British police official and an FBI agent spoke to the Hunts
after the Monroe County Medical Examiner's Office determined through
dental records and X-rays Saturday that the remains sent to the
family were not those of Karen Hunt.
   Ms. Hunt, a student at Syracuse University, would have celebrated
her 21st birthday Saturday.
   ``The British official just wanted to apologize,'' Hunt said.
``He offered a wholehearted apology and promised they were doing
everything possible to rectify the situation.''
   ``They really wouldn't commit to anything else,'' he said. ``I
got the feeling they don't want to tell us anything unless they're
100 percent sure.''
   He said he didn't know the name or title of the Scotland Yard
official.
   Gregory Jones, an FBI spokesman in Washington, confirmed that a
British police official met with Hunt, but said he had no details on
the misidentification. Jones said he didn't know what will be done
with the body sent to the Hunts.
   Officials in Washington ``have no idea'' where Ms. Hunt's body
is, Jones said.
   Karen Hunt was returning to New York after completing a
one-semester program in Syracuse University's Division of
International Programs Abroad in London. She was one of 35 such
students to die in the crash.
</TEXT>

<HEAD>Brand New Boeing 737 Crashes With Engine Failure; 43 Dead</HEAD>
<HEAD>Eds: LEADS with 14 grafs to UPDATE with flight recorders found, officials
say both engines may have failed. Pickup 14th pvs, `The plane...</HEAD>
<HEAD>LaserPhotos</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By ROBERT BARR</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>KEGWORTH, England (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Investigators today recovered the flight
recorders from the wreckage of a new Boeing 737 that crashed along a
highway after one engine, and possibly both, failed. At least 43
people were killed and 83 injured.
   Firefighters pumped foam onto the crumpled jet to prevent seeping
aviation fuel from catching fire. The Belfast-bound jet carrying 126
people broke into three pieces on the edge of Britain's main
north-south highway, the M1, in central England on Sunday night.
   The twin-engine Boeing 737-400 narrowly missed the small town of
Kegworth and plowed into an embankment just a few hundred yards
short of the runway as the pilot struggled to make an emergency
landing at East Midland Airport, 100 miles north of London.
   One engine was on fire, and Transport Secretary Paul Channon said
it ``certainly looks probable'' there was a problem with the second
engine.
   British Midland Airways said sabotage was not suspected in the
crash, which came less than three weeks after a bomb blew apart Pan
Am Flight 103 over a Scottish village, Lockerbie, killing all 259
people on board and 11 on the ground.
   Witnesses and a radio operator who monitored the pilot's final
exchanges with air traffic controllers Sunday also said both engines
appeared to have failed.
   British Midland said the jet was delivered just 12 weeks ago and
had flown less than 500 hours. It grounded its other 737-400 for
inspection.
   ``At the moment it does seem to be a technical problem,'' said
Paul Beaver, a managing editor of aviation for Jane's publications.
``I think they have ruled out pilot error and almost definitely
ruled out sabotage.''
   Beaver, interviewed on ABC-TV's ``Good Morning America,'' said
the chances of both engines failing were ``something like a 10
million to one chance, and of course sometimes those chances come
up.''
   At least four British soldiers serving in Northern Ireland were
among the 43 killed, police said.
   ``It's amazing'' no motorists or nearby residents were killed,
Leicestershire Assistant Chief Constable Tony Butler told a news
conference today. ``It's a very busy road.''
   Channon said the fast response of emergency services saved lives.
   ``The airport fire brigade was there within minutes ... a number
of people are living as a result.''
   Police said the last person was taken from the wreckage more than
seven hours after the crash. They said it took 2{ hours to extract
the pilot, Kevin Hunt. A spokeswoman for the Leicester Royal
Infirmary said Hunt was in poor condition with multiple injuries.
   The plane took off from London's Heathrow Airport at 7:52 p.m.,
and about eight minutes later reported ``severe vibrations in one of
the engines,'' said airline chairman Michael Bishop.
   The pilot reported an engine on fire, then said he had it under
control and asked for clearance to make an emergency landing at East
Midlands Airport, the airline's home base 100 miles north of London.
   Witnesses said the left engine was on fire as the plane crashed
about a half-mile shy of the runway. Ham radio operator Mervyn
Solloway said that from what he could hear, both engines were in
trouble.
   ``Suddenly there was, not a shouted message, but a bit of a
frantic one to say, `We've got problems with the other engine.' That
was the last I heard from the aircraft,'' he said.
   The aircraft sheared tree tops and plowed into a steep embankment
by the M1 highway outside the town of Kegworth. A piece of the jet
lodged in the highway divider and bodies and personal belongings
spilled from the plane.
   Motorists swerved in terror and some cars collided, police said.
The tail section embedded in the ground while the fuselage tore
apart, the nose section coming to rest in a bramble thicket.
   Dozens of fire engines, ambulances, residents and motorists
rushed to the scene. The engine fire was quickly extinguished, and
rescuers cut passengers loose. Surgeons performed amputations on the
spot.
   A local Episcopal vicar, the Rev. Michael Brandon, recited the
Lord's Prayer as a man died, clutching his hand.
   ``The whole plane is carnage,'' said Vic Milner, an Automobile
Association worker. ``People were strapped into their seats, dead in
the tangled body of the plane.''
   Several people in Kegworth, a town of 2,000 residents, said that
when the plane crashed, their first thought was of Lockerbie, the
little Scottish town that bore the brunt of the crash of Pan Am
Flight 103 on Dec. 21.
   All 259 people aboard that Boeing 747 were killed, along with 11
Lockerbie residents. Investigators said a bomb blew up the plane.
   In Sunday's crash, ``there nothing to indicate (sabotage)
whatsoever,'' said the airline's marketing director, Colin Roberts.
   David Learmount of the respected journal Flight International
said the British Midland plane must have developed ``more than just
a simple engine failure or the pilot could have gotten the plane
down safely.''
   He told British Broadcasting Corp. television said the Boeing
737-400 has ``the most brilliant safety record in the world today.''
   ``If an airplane's engine catches fire one of the reasons why is
that the engine, which has very fast spinning parts, literally
breaks apart and bits come out through the casing and this can cause
a fire,'' he said.
   In Seattle, Boeing spokesman Jack Gamble said Sunday's crash was
the first involving a 737-400. The model had its first flight in
March and airlines are flying 17 of the jets, he said.
</TEXT>

<HEAD>Woman Who Staged Mock Funeral Dies of Cancer</HEAD>
<HEAD>Eds: SUBS 2nd graf to restore dropped time element.</HEAD>
<DATELINE>MILWAUKEE (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Carrie Munson, a social worker who staged and
attended her own mock funeral 2{ months ago, has died of cancer of
the pancreas. She was 62.
   Munson had said she held the mock funeral, which drew nationwide
attention, to celebrate life and show that she did not fear death.
She died Sunday.
   The Rev. R.L. Lathan, who delivered the eulogy at the memorial
service in October, said two companies have been seeking the right
to publish the biography of Munson, who struggled to overcome heroin
addiction and became a social worker. An independent filmmaker is
also pursuing the rights to a movie about her life.
   ``She wanted to prove to the coming generation that the evils of
drugs could be defeated,'' Lathan said. ``That's what kept her so
fired up to fight it. She was real determined.''
   She had been diagnosed as having inoperable cancer of the
pancreas in October 1987.
   Munson said at the time of her mock funeral that she had been
addicted to drugs for years before turning her life around and
earning a degree from the University of Wisconsin-Milwaukee in 1975.

<FIRST>r p PM-DNCChairman     01-09 0480</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-DNC Chairman,0494</SECOND>
<HEAD>Five Seeking Democrats' Chairman Job Meet With Southern Leaders</HEAD>
<DATELINE>ATLANTA (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   All five candidates for chairman of the Democratic
National Committee played to their Southern audience at a weekend
meeting, with the stress on luring moderate and conservative
Southern whites back to the party.
   The five candidates met about 100 committee members and party
activists Sunday.
   ``I don't want to run a party that writes off the South,'' said
Washington lawyer Ronald H. Brown, who has served in top party posts
and served as Jesse Jackson's 1988 convention manager. ``It doesn't
make sense. You can't do it that way.''
   Brown and Michigan party Chairman Richard N. Wiener, who is
president of the Association of State Democratic Chairs, are
considered favorites. But neither candidate has a majority of the
404-member DNC, and party leaders believe one of three dark horses
could win the Feb. 10 election.
   They are former Reps. Michael D. Barnes of Maryland, James R.
Jones of Oklahoma and James V. Stanton of Ohio.
   While many party chairmen in the West and Midwest have rallied
around Wiener, most Southern chairmen have remained uncommitted.
   Much of the talk Sunday concerned changing the public's
perception of the Democratic message. President-elect George Bush
swept the South in the November elections, just as most Republican
candidates have done for a generation.
   ``The perception is that we are to the left, are much more
liberal, than the mainstream voters,'' Jones said. ``Unless and
until we recognize that, unless and until we bring the party to a
position that is viewed as centrist and representing the vast
majority of this very diverse country, we're not going to win
elections.''
   Jones advocated a three-year advertising campaign that would
attempt to ``make it respectable to be a Democrat again.''
   Wiener said Republicans have put six hurdles in the path of
Democrats running for president: the issues of national defense,
patriotism, crime, gun control, school prayer and abortion.
   ``We don't have to be to the right of the Republicans on those
issues,'' he said. ``But we need to pass a certain threshold on
those issues.''
   He suggested imitating the GOP practice of spending heavily on
issues research, to identify Democratic strengths and Republican
weaknesses.
   Southerners remained uncommitted after the meeting.
   James Van Hecke Jr., the North Carolina party chairman, said the
Southern chairmen may hold a telephone conference later this week to
explore the possibility of a group endorsement.
   Several of the Southern party leaders in the audience said they
appreciated the lip service paid to regional concerns but heard few
specific solutions.
   ``Everybody has said to us for years that the South is the key,
and not one of these candidates disagreed,'' said Alan Diamonstein,
chairman of the DNC's Southern caucus. ``But you can't keep saying
that mathematically the South is the key while not saying how to do
anything about it.''
</TEXT>

<DATELINE>WASHINGTON (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Here is the text of the budget message which
President Reagan sent to Congress on Monday.
</TEXT>
<HEAD>To the Speaker of the House of Representatives and the President
of the Senate:</HEAD>
<TEXT>
   Eight years ago many in this country were concerned about the
future of our economy, our government and, indeed, the nation itself.
   Unemployment was high and rising.
   Inflation and interest rates were reaching record levels.
   Our nation's defense capabilities had been weakened by neglect.
   The international prestige of the U.S. was at low ebb.
   To resolve the economic problems then facing us, our
administration proposed a recovery program centering on four
fundamental principles:
   _Reduce personal and business tax rates.
   _Reduce the rate of growth of federal spending.
   _Reduce the federal regulatory burden by eliminating unnecessary
restrictions while protecting the public's interest and safety.
   _Support a moderate and steady monetary policy to bring inflation
under control.
   To rebuild our defense capabilities and restore America's
standing in the world we proposed expanded national security and
international programs.
   We also initiated the largest management improvement program ever
attempted in order to restore the proper relationships among the
federal, state and local governments, and the private sector; to
eliminate waste in federal programs; and to introduce management
controls and efficiencies, while improving services.
   Today, the American people can be proud of the progress that has
been made on each of these fronts.
   As a result of this progress, America is internally stronger,
internationally more secure, and stands taller in the eyes of the
world than it did eight years ago.
</TEXT>
<HEAD>EIGHT YEARS OF ACCOMPLISHMENT</HEAD>
<TEXT>
   Working together, we have accomplished much over the last eight
years.
</TEXT>

<HEAD>Police Report ``Horrific Massacres'' In Shiite Fighting; 25 Dead</HEAD>
<HEAD>Eds: Leads with 7 grafs to UPDATE casualties. Pickup 9th pvs, `Amal
claimed...</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By RODEINA KENAAN</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>KFAR MELKI, Lebanon (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Syrian-backed Shiite Moslem fighters
recaptured villages in south Lebanon today from pro-Iranian rivals.
Police reported ``horrific massacres'' and said at least 80 people
were killed in a 24-hour period.
   The Amal militia claimed its fighters had overrun four of five
villages seized Sunday by the Iranian-backed Hezbollah, or Party of
God.
   But reporters who drove into the battle zone near Israel's
self-designated ``security zone'' on Lebanon's southern border said
Amal had retaken only three villages. Fierce fighting raged around
the other two.
   Police said at least 80 people had been killed and 200 wounded
since Sunday morning.
   A police spokesman in Sidon said many victims had been hacked to
death.
   ``Others had their throats cut and some were headless. Many were
riddled with bullets,'' said the spokesman, who cannot be named
under standing regulations. ``Horrific massacres have been
committed.''
   The casualties were the highest 24-hour toll since the power
struggle for Lebanon's 1 million Shiites, the country's largest
sect, flared in April. The latest round of battles began Dec. 31.
   Amal claimed its fighters recaptured four of the villages along
the main road from Sidon, 25 miles south of Beirut, to Israel's
security zone on Lebanon's southern border.
   Reporters said Amal drove Hezbollah out of the battered villages
of Kfar Hatta, Kfar Melki and Kfar Beit in hand-to-hand fighting
from late Sunday to dawn Monday.
   Amal military commanders in Kfar Melki said about 1,000
militiamen and several Syrian-supplied T-54 tanks were attacking the
other two hamlets, Kfar Filla and Jbaa, where an estimated 500
Hezbollah fighters were holed up.
   Cars hit by rocket-propelled grenades smoldered in the streets of
Kfar Hatta and Kfar Melki, whose 8,000 inhabitants spent the night
hiding in apple and olive groves.
   Aisha Barhoum, 35, found her one-story house in Kfar Melki gutted
when she returned: ``They've burned my house, but God has burned
them in Amal's wrath.''
   As the fighting raged, she said: ``We've been liberated from
Hezbolshaitan.'' That's an Arabic pun on Hezbollah's name, meaning
``Party of Satan.''
   Amal and Hezbollah accused each other of ``atrocities and summary
executions'' in the fighting.
   Iran and Syria, Iran's main Arab ally in the 8-year Iran-Iraq
war, have been trying to rein in their feuding Lebanese proxies but
the fighting has raged despite appeals from President Ali Khamenei
of Iran and Syrian leader Hafez Assad.
   In Beirut, Syrian troops with rocket-propelled grenade launchers
patrolled two neighborhoods where Amal and Hezbollah clashed for two
hours Sunday.
   In April, Amal drove Hezbollah fighters from the south in a
four-day battle in which 62 people were killed and 150 wounded.
   Hezbollah retaliated in May, pushing Amal out of most of Beirut's
southern slums, a traditional Shiite stronghold, in a three-week
onslaught. Nearly 300 people were killed and 1,000 wounded.
   The fighting stopped when Syria, whose army controls security in
Moslem west Beirut, sent 4,500 troops into the slums.
   But the bloodletting broke out again in south Beirut in November
and spilled over into west Beirut. Forty people were killed and 87
wounded in six days of clashes.
</TEXT>

<HEAD>Tourists Snare Man Suspected in Motel Robbery Attempt</HEAD>
<DATELINE>DAYTONA BEACH, Fla. (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   A would-be thief got more than he
bargained for when he trired to rob an elderly couple in a motel
room and was tackled by the man, then chased and captured by a crowd
of tourists, police said.
   Michael Lassiter, 33, of Daytona Beach was being held without
bond today in Volusia County Branch Jail on assault and robbery
charges following the Sunday morning incident.
   According to police, Lassiter entered the motel room of Howard
Douglas, 69, of Milwaukee and his wife, Evelyn, and demanded their
money.
   Douglas leaped out of the bathroom and tackled Lassister, police
said. Meanwhile, Mrs. Douglas yelled to a crowd at the pool deck.
She also took off her rings and threw them to a woman by the pool.
   Lassiter, who is 5-11 and 185 pounds, fled after a brief struggle
with Douglas.
   ``I was 5 feet 7 inches tall and 150 pounds, but when you get
near 70, I think you begin to shrink,'' Douglas said later. ``He
looked big when he was coming at me, but he looked a lot smaller
next to the police.''
   Lassiter reportedly headed toward the beach, with a crowd of
tourists _ including the Douglases' 38-year-old son, Craig _ in
pursuit.
   As Kempton Rothpletz, 24, of Dallas left his car to investigate
the commotion, Lassiter leaped into the vehicle and began to drive
off.
   But Lassiter's bad luck continued as Rothpletz jumped back into
the moving car and managed to force it into several parked cars.
Rothpletz suffered scrapes and bruises to his legs when they were
pinned by the car door, police said.
   Lassiter was captured by the group of tourists as he tried to run
away.
   He was charged with strongarm robbery, auto theft, aggravated
assault and burglary. A check showed that he also was wanted for
armed robbery and theft, police said.

<FIRST>r w PM-FreshmanClass 1stLd-Writethru a0412 01-09 0799</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Freshman Class, 1st Ld-Writethru, a0412,780</SECOND>
<HEAD>Class Of '92 Getting Less Financial Help, But More Plan Advanced
Study</HEAD>
<HEAD>Eds: Inserts new 4th graf, ``At the,'' to raise reference to depression,
stress</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By JILL LAWRENCE</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>AP Education Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>WASHINGTON (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Entering college freshmen are getting less aid
from the federal government and are increasingly interested in
making their educations pay off in cold cash, according to a survey
of more than 300,000 students.
   The annual survey nonetheless found that students entering
college in 1988 were more likely than their 1987 counterparts to
want to pursue graduate study despite the financial obstacles.
   And although students are more interested in making money, they
seem less interested in business careers as a means of reaching that
goal, the survey said.
   At the same time, a record proportion of freshmen reported
themselves feeling frequently depressed. Stress over paying for
college and attaining advanced degrees were possible contributing
factors.
   The findings released Sunday by the American Council on Education
and the Higher Education Research Institute at the University of
California at Los Angeles are based on questionnaires filled out by
308,007 freshmen entering 585 two-and four-year colleges and
universities. The results were adjusted to reflect the total
population of 1.64 million first-time, full-time entering students
and have a margin of error of plus or minus two points.
   Only 15.6 percent of freshmen entering school last fall received
Pell grants, the government's major aid program for low- and
middle-income students, the survey found. That was the lowest level
since the survey began 23 years ago and less than half the 31.5
percent who received Pell grants in 1980.
   Freshman participation in other federal aid programs also fell,
while students reported greater reliance on family contributions,
savings, loans and institutional grants.
   The amount of Pell grant aid awarded to students actually rose
nearly 57 percent from 1980 to 1988, or 17 percent taking inflation
into account, according to The College Board, a New York-based
lobbying group.
   But during the same period, the aid was more sharply aimed at the
very neediest post-secondary students, many of whom shun college in
favor of vocational schools that teach cosmetology, art, drama, auto
mechanics, business skills or other trades.
   Victoria Tripp, an Education Department spokeswoman, said 11
percent of Pell money went to vocational students in 1980, compared
with 27 percent in 1988. ``That figure will increase to one-third in
the early 1990s,'' she predicted. ``The money is targeted to those
students and there has been a proliferation of those schools.''
   Despite the falloff in college aid, a record proportion of
freshmen said they were interested in pursuing advanced degrees _
58.7 percent, compared with 48.3 percent in 1980.
   ``The rising degree aspirations suggest that a growing proportion
of students feel the bachelor's degree will not be an adequate
credential in the job market during the next decade and into the
next century,'' said Kenneth C. Green, associate director of the
survey.
   In line with that, a record 72.6 percent of the freshmen
indicated ``making more money'' was a very important factor in their
decision to attend college. By contrast, the proportion who said
they were going to college ``to gain a general education'' declined
for the 11th straight year, to 60.1 percent.
   Examining personal habits, the survey also found that a 22-year
decline in smoking may be reversing itself. In 1966, 16.6 percent of
the freshmen said they were frequent smokers. That fell steadily to
8.9 percent in 1987, but rose to 10.1 percent among those entering
college last fall.
   A record high percentage of freshmen (10.5 percent) said they
frequently felt depressed, while there was no real change in those
rating their emotional health as above average (56.1 percent, as
opposed to 56.8 percent in 1987).
   Survey director Alexander Astin of UCLA said the survey revealed
several potential sources of increased stress for students,
including concern about paying for college; increased interest in
advanced degrees; and tougher competition for admission to selective
institutions.
   On the career front, the survey found that freshman interest in
entering the business world to be leveling off _ 24.6 percent in
1987 and 23.6 percent in 1988 _ after two decades of steady gains.
The results ``suggest that student interest in business careers may
have reached a plateau after the dramatic increases of the 1970s and
1980s,'' survey officials said.
   Student interest in teaching rose from 8.1 percent in 1987 to 8.8
percent in 1988, reflecting a trend that started in 1983 after
interest had bottomed out at 4.7 percent in 1982.
   But interest in science careers, steadily falling in popularity
for the last 20 years, remained low. Only 5.8 percent of the
freshmen said they planned to pursue science majors, compared with 6
percent in 1987 and 11.5 percent in 1973.
</TEXT>
