<FIRST>u w BC-BudgetText 1stadd   01-09 0373</FIRST>
<SECOND>BC-Budget Text, 1st add,370</SECOND>
<HEAD>WASHN: eight years.</HEAD>
<HEAD>The Economy</HEAD>
<TEXT>
   _The current economic expansion, now in its 74th month, has
outlasted all previous peacetime expansions in U.S. history.
Business investment and exports are rising, and economic growth is
expected to continue into the 1990s.
   _Since this expansion began, 19 million new jobs have been
created, while the unemployment rate has fallen by more than 5
percentage points _ to 5.4 percent, the lowest level in 14 years.
   _Inflation, which averaged 10.4 percent annually during the four
years before our administration began, has averaged less than a
third of that during the past six years.
   _Real after-tax personal income has risen 24 percent since 1982,
increasing our overall standard of living.
</TEXT>
<HEAD>Taxes and Regulations</HEAD>
<TEXT>
   _Between 1981 and 1987, changes in the federal tax code have made
the tax laws more equitable, cut income tax rates, and eliminated
federal income taxes for 4.3 million low-income individuals and
families.
   _Since 1981, the time spent by the public filling out forms
required by the federal government has been cut by 600 million hours
annually, and the number of pages of regulations published annually
in the Federal Register has been reduced by over 45 percent.
</TEXT>
<HEAD>Budget</HEAD>
<TEXT>
   _The growth in domestic spending has been slowed, and the budget
priorities have been shifted to those functions the federal
government should provide, such as national defense, basic
scientific research, and protecting the rights of all citizens.
   _The Social Security system has been rescued from the brink of
insolvency and made sound into the next century.
   _The runaway growth of spending for means-tested entitlement
programs that occurred in the 1970s has been curbed. Eligibility
rules have been tightened to retarget benefits to the needy. Basic
benefits for the poor, the elderly, and others in need of federal
assistance have been maintained.
   _We have begun the process of putting other entitlement programs
on a more rational basis, including Medicare hospital insurance,
which was converted to a system that encourages efficiency and lower
costs.
</TEXT>
<HEAD>Defense and International Affairs</HEAD>
<TEXT>
   _Our defenses have been strengthened. Weapons systems have been
modernized and upgraded. We are recruiting and retaining higher
caliber military personnel. The readiness, training, and morale of
our troops have been improved significantly.
</TEXT>

<SECOND>PM-France-Iran, 1st Ld-Writethru, a0514,0509</SECOND>
<HEAD>Iran Foreign Minister Says Peace Process `Volatile'</HEAD>
<HEAD>With PM-Chemical Conference, Bjt</HEAD>
<HEAD>Eds: INSERTS 5 grafs after graf 4th, ``The situation ... our territory,''
to UPDATE with Tehran Radio commentary. SUBS graf 5 pvs to conform. PICKS
UP graf 6 pvs, ``Iraq has ...''</HEAD>
<DATELINE>PARIS (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Iran's foreign minister said today that prospects
for success in Iran-Iraq peace talks are dim, but he said his
country is ready to agree to an international ban on chemical
weapons whether or not Iraq signs.
   Foreign Minister Ali Akbar Velayati, attending an international
conference on chemical weapons, said he had met with U.N.
Secretary-General Javier Perez de Cuellar to discuss the 8-year-old
Iran-Iraq war.
   A cease-fire began Aug. 20 and negotiations aimed at achieving a
lasting peace began a few days later.
   ``The situation remains volatile and prospects for progress are
dim,'' Velayati told reporters. ``Five months after the cease-fire,
Iraq is still in our territory.''
   Tehran Radio said today, ``The Islamic Republic has always
demonstrated its willingness to participate in the talks, but until
(while) Iraq continues to introduce subterfuges, a resumption of
talks will achieve nothing.''
   The commentary, monitored in Nicosia, gave the strongest
indication yet that Iran may not return to the negotiating table
without guarantees of concrete results.
   The U.N. Security Council and all countries which support its
cease-fire resolution ``must not expect Iran to be optimistic about
the talks until (while) Iraq still refuses to withdraw from Iranian
territory,'' said the broadcast.
   Tehran's accusation that Baghdad is dragging its feet comes
several days after Iraqi President Saddam Hussein claimed Iran was
massing troops along the 730-mile border and probably planning ``new
aggression.'' He also accused Tehran of stalling in the peace talks.
   In Paris, Velayati said Perez de Cuellar is sending high-level
envoys to Baghdad and Tehran soon, and ``we welcome them.''
   Iraq has used chemical weapons, and Velayati called his
countrymen ``the most conspicuous victims'' of violations of the
1925 Geneva Protocol, which bans the use of such weapons. Iraq
admitted using chemical weapons in the war but said Iran used them
first.
   Velayati repeated Iranian claims that his country did not use the
weapons.
   Asked if Iran would be willing to sign an accord expanding the
chemical weapons ban if Iraq refused to sign, Velayati said: ``We
are ready to participate in any global or regional activities on
chemical weapons.''
   Velayati also said he met early today with William Waldegrave,
head of the British delegation, to discuss British hostages being
held in Lebanon.
   He said Iran had been asked for help and that in the past they
had tried to assist ``France, West Germany, even the United States''
in obtaining freedom for hostages held in Lebanon.
   ``We do hope all nationals will be released,'' he said.
   Fifteen foreigners, including nine Americans, are held hostage in
Lebanon. The longest held is Terry Anderson, chief Middle East
correspondent for The Associated Press, who was abducted March 16,
1985.
</TEXT>

<HEAD>Seven Reported Wounded, Strike Shuts Territories</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By HAITHAM HAMAD</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>NABLUS, Occupied West Bank (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Seven Palestinians were
reported shot and wounded in clashes with Israeli troops today, and
a general strike shut down the occupied West Bank and Gaza Strip.
   The daily Haaretz newspaper said Defense Minister Yitzhak Rabin
had renewed secret contacts with Palestinians to discuss holding
municipal elections in the occupied territories for the first time
since 1976.
   Meetings at Rabin's Tel Aviv office were held in secret to
protect Arab participants from attacks by Palestinian nationalists,
the Hebrew-language paper said.
   The PLO-backed underground leadership of the 13-month uprising
rejected Israeli moves for elections and called for escalation of
the Arab revolt in a leaflet distributed in the territories.
   The army imposed curfews on four Gaza Strip refugee camps and two
neighborhoods of Gaza City and Rafah, confining 135,000 Palestinians
to their homes.
   A military spokesman confirmed four Palestinians were wounded
during clashes with troops in the West Bank refugee camp of Farah,
near Nablus. He said the army was checking other reported casualties.
   Arab hospital officials said two of the wounded were in critical
condition with bullet wounds to the stomach.
   Three other teen-agers were hospitalized with less serious bullet
wounds, according to hospital officials.
   Also today, Arab residents of the West Bank village of Kfar Nakif
threw a firebomb at an Israeli car, which exploded but caused no
damage, Arab reports said. The attack prompted a raid on the village
by a group of Jewish settlers, who threw rocks that damaged two
Palestinian cars, the reports said.
   The army said it was investigating the report.
   In Gaza City, residents hung outlawed Palestinian flags and
photographs of PLO chief Yasser Arafat from electricity lines,
according to Arab reporters.
   Troops used tear gas to break up a demonstration in the West Bank
refugee camp of Dheishe, near Bethlehem, after youths began stoning
soldiers guarding the camp entrance.
   By official count, 348 palestinians and 15 Israelis have been
killed in the uprising.
   The strike call by the PLO-backed leadership came after a
shutdown was ordered by a rival Islamic fundamentalist group, Hamas,
which means ``Zeal'' in Arabic.
   Past Hamas strikes have sparked confrontations with PLO
supporters who demanded merchants not obey the orders. But
Palestinians close to the leadership said the group decided to avoid
confrontation this time.
   ``The United Leadership has started to realize that Hamas has the
power to enforce a strike and they wanted credit for today's
strike,'' said a pro-PLO activist who gave his name only as Ghassan.
   Hamas supporters warned merchants with graffiti in the West Bank
city of Nablus. ``Any merchant who opens his shop will find it
torched to the ground,'' said the warnings painted in red.
   Other slogans signed by Hamas and the extremist Popular Front for
the Liberation of Palestine-General Command criticized recent moves
for a negotiated settlement of the Arab-Israeli conflict.
   ``No to negotiation, no to recognition of Israel,'' said one
slogan.
</TEXT>

<SECOND>BC-Budget Text, 2nd add,430</SECOND>
<HEAD>WASHN: improved significantly.</HEAD>
<TEXT>
    _As a result of our greater strength, we were able to negotiate
with the Soviet Union a verifiable treaty that completely eliminates
an entire class of nuclear missiles.
   _We began the Strategic Defense Initiative research and
technology program that offers our best hope of a safer world in
which our security, and that of our allies, no longer rests on
deterrence through the threat of nuclear retaliation, but on
defenses that threaten no one.
   _Our willingness to defend freedom throughout the world has met
with success in the spread of democracy and in turning back the tide
of communist expansion.
</TEXT>
<HEAD>Management of the Government</HEAD>
<TEXT>
   _Federal agencies undertook a major management improvement
program, ``Reform '88,'' to carry out the cash, credit, and
financial operations of the federal government in a more
businesslike manner, and to reduce waste, fraud, and abuse.
   _Functions that were pre-empted by the federal government are
being transferred back to the private sector or to state and local
governments.
   _Greater use is being made of cost sharing and user fees,
shifting the cost of projects and programs where appropriate to
those who benefit from them.
</TEXT>
<HEAD>The Federal Deficit</HEAD>
<TEXT>
   The one area in which I have been persistently disappointed
throughout my term of office has been in the efforts to bring the
budget under control.
   Time and again I have proposed measures to help curb federal
domestic program spending. Time and again these proposals have been
rejected by Congress.
   The reasons for the rise in the federal deficit in the early
1980s are simple.
   First, we experienced one of the most severe recessions of the
postwar period.
   It has been estimated that 81 percent _ over $640 billion _ of
the growth of the deficit over the 1981-1986 levels originally
projected in my March 1981 budget was attributable to that recession.
   The second reason is that, even after including necessary
increases for defense, my March 1981 budget called for net spending
reductions totaling $331 billion over five years; but Congress
approved less than 40 percent of those reductions.
   Wasteful programs continued to be funded.
   The necessary reductions have still not been made.
   If the deficit is not curbed by continuing to limit the appetite
of government, we put in jeopardy all that we worked so hard over
the years to achieve.
   Large deficits brought on by excessive domestic spending undercut
the incentives to work and save by absorbing the savings that would
otherwise lead to productive investment.
   We cannot allow this to happen.
</TEXT>

<HEAD>WASHN: continuing prosperity.</HEAD>
<HEAD>FUNDING NATIONAL PRIORITIES</HEAD>
<TEXT>
   To address urgent national priorities within the deficit limit
set by the G-R-H Act, my budget proposes that some programs _ such
as those for AIDS research and prevention, drug enforcement, and
technology development _ receive significant funding increases,
while others are reduced, reformed, or, in some cases, terminated.
</TEXT>
<HEAD>Defense</HEAD>
<TEXT>
   Maintaining peace and protecting our country are the foremost
responsibilities of the federal government.
   Defense budget authority declined in real (inflation-adjusted)
terms for the fourth straight year with funding of $299 billion for
1989.
   This trend cannot continue without severe impact on combat
readiness.
   Therefore, my budget requests defense funding of $315 billion in
budget authority and $303 billion in outlays for 1990, and $331
billion in budget authority and $314 billion in outlays in 1991.
   These amounts provide 2 percent annual real growth in budget
authority over the 1989 level, bringing it back up to the 1984 level
in inflation-adjusted terms by 1991.
   The budget also projects 2 percent real growth in these programs
in future years.
   We must continue to maintain our nuclear deterrent.
   For 1990, the budget proposes $9 billion for atomic energy
defense programs, a $0.9 billion increase over 1989.
   A total of $2.8 billion is dedicated to the modernization of the
nuclear materials production complex and to increase environmental
cleanup and waste management efforts.
</TEXT>
<HEAD>International</HEAD>
<TEXT>
   To consolidate and build on the foreign policy gains of the past
eight years, additional funds are needed for international affairs
that would promote our foreign policy and national security
interests in the Middle East, Central America, and elsewhere.
   A special program is proposed to foster strong economic growth in
the Philippines to support that country's return to democracy.
   Other increases in foreign aid would pay arrearages on
contributions to multilateral lending institutions and make payments
to the United Nations and related agencies.
</TEXT>
<HEAD>Drug Abuse and Law Enforcement</HEAD>
<TEXT>
   Our fight against drug abuse must continue, as well as our
efforts to protect the individual against crime:
   _For drug law enforcement, prevention, and treatment programs, I
propose $5.7 billion in 1990, an increase of $164 million over 1989.
This funding, together with the new authorities and sanctions
contained in the Anti-Drug Abuse Act of 1988, will enable us to move
toward our goal of a drug-free America.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r w PM-Scotus-RevenueSharing     01-09 0269</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Scotus-Revenue Sharing,250</SECOND>
<HEAD>Court Bars Local Governments From Receiving Some Revenue Sharing
Funds</HEAD>
<DATELINE>WASHINGTON (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Supreme Court today barred local
governments from receiving $180 million in federal revenue sharing
funds withheld by the Reagan administration.
   The court, without comment, left intact a ruling that the money
properly was withheld under budget cuts imposed by the so-called
Gramm-Rudman Act.
   Congress appropriated more than $4 billion for revenue sharing
for the 1986 fiscal year.
   After Gramm-Rudman was enacted _ a law aimed at eliminating the
federal budget deficit by 1991 _ President Reagan ordered $180
million in revenue sharing funds withheld in 1986.
   Later that year, Congress passed and Reagan signed a law
repealing the revenue sharing program.
   Representatives of local governments in early 1987 asked the
Reagan administration to pay the $180 million that had been held
back the previous year. The administration refused the request.
   The local governments then went to federal court and a judge
ordered the government to pay the $180 million.
   But the U.S. Circuit Court of Appeals here ruled last March that
the administration acted properly.
   The appeals court said the intent of Gramm-Rudman was not merely
to freeze budget cuts temporarily.
   ``Merely freezing the fiscal year 1986 entitlements and
resuscitating them in fiscal year 1987... would not result in any
meaningful deficit reduction and could be characterized by critics
to be a sham,'' the appeals court said.
   Barring the local governments from receiving the money ``may be
harsh,'' the appeals court added. ``But we believe it is consistent
with congressional intent.''
   The case is National Association of Counties vs. Brady, 88-505.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>u w PM-Scotus-PhonePorn     01-09 0557</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Scotus-Phone Porn,520</SECOND>
<HEAD>Justices to Consider Ban on Sex Calls in California Case</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By RICHARD CARELLI</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>WASHINGTON (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Supreme Court today agreed to decide
whether Congress may outlaw all sexually explicit telephone dial-up
message services, known as ``dial-a-porn.''
   The justices will rule, sometime by July, on the validity of a
1988 law that sought to ban all obscene and indecent messages
available through dial-a-porn services.
   A federal judge in California ruled that the law could be applied
to ban all obscene messages, but he struck down the law's ban on
messages deemed merely indecent.
   U.S. District Judge A. Wallace Tashima in Los Angeles based his
July 19 ruling on past Supreme Court decisions that excluded obscene
material _ but not all sexually explicit or indecent material _ from
constitutional free-speech protections.
   The Reagan administration appealed directly to the Supreme Court,
challenging the part of the July ruling that protects indecent
material.
   Sable Communications of California Inc., a major purveyor of
dial-a-porn services in that state, filed a counter-appeal
challenging the part of Tashima's ruling that allows the ban on
materials judged to be obscene.
   Today, the justices granted review to both appeals.
   Twice in 1988 the justices had refused to review dial-a-porn
controversies, in cases from Arizona and New York.
   Obscenity is a legal term that does not apply to all sexually
explicit material.
   The Supreme Court since 1973 has defined obscenity as a work that
appeals to a morbid interest in sex with patently offensive
depictions or descriptions of sexual conduct, and one that taken as
a whole has no serious literary, artistic, political or scientific
value.
   Sexually explicit material, often called pornography, may or may
not be legally obscene.
   In 1978, the high court drew a distinction between obscene and
``indecent'' expression when it allowed federal regulators to
restrict the broadcast of indecent language.
   Upholding the government's power to punish use of ``dirty words''
over the airwaves at times when children might be listening, the
court seemed to adopt the Federal Communications Commission's
definition of indecent _ ``patently offensive references to
excretory and sexual organs or activities.''
   Congress in 1983 imposed restrictions on the dial-a-porn business
but did not seek a total ban until enacting the 1988 law.
   ``Congress concluded that there was no sufficiently effective
measure to eliminate access of children to dial-a-porn short of an
outright ban,'' Justice Department lawyers told the court.
   They said the court's 1978 decision restricting the broadcasting
of indecent language ``suggests that (the 1988 law) is consistent
with the First Amendment,'' the constitutional protection of freedom
of speech.
   The government's appeal acknowledged, however, that the FCC's
general counsel has said the commission believes the 1978 Supreme
Court ruling does not authorize a total ban on merely indecent
language.
   Lawyers for Sable Communications argued that the entire 1988 law
should be struck down.
   Dial-a-porn companies make their services available to callers
through 976-exchange numbers and AT&amp;T's area code 900 long-distance
lines.
   The telephone company typically charges callers according to
per-call rates, retaining a portion of the charges and giving the
remainder to the dial-a-porn company.
   The 976 and 900 exchanges also are used for other,
non-controversial types of messages such as sports scores, time
checks and stock market reports.
   The cases are FCC vs. Sable Communications, 88-525, and Sable
Communications vs. FCC, 88-515.
</TEXT>
</DOC>

<FIRST>r w PM-Scotus-TaxOpinions     01-09 0376</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Scotus-Tax Opinions,360</SECOND>
<HEAD>Court to Decide if Justice Must Supply Magazine with Tax Documents</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By RICHARD CARELLI</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>WASHINGTON (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Supreme Court today agreed to decide
whether the Justice Department must supply a weekly tax magazine
with copies of all federal trial court opinions and orders in tax
cases.
   The justices said they will review a ruling that the Freedom of
Information Act imposes such an obligation on the Justice
Department's tax division.
   Government lawyers, warning of ``enormous administrative costs
and burdens'' at taxpayer expense, contend that the 1966 law imposes
no such obligation.
   Tax Analysts, a Washington-based weekly magazine reporting
legislative, judicial and regulatory developments in federal
taxation, first invoked the FOIA in 1984 in seeking timely copies of
federal court opinions and orders from the Justice Department.
   Such opinions and orders are available to the public from the
individual courts but the magazine had difficulty in obtaining them
quickly.
   The Justice Department's tax division turned down the magazine's
FOIA requests, and a federal trial judge upheld the denial.
   But the U.S. Circuit Court of Appeals here ruled last April 29
that the government must comply with the magazine's FOIA requests.
   ``Imposing an enormous and costly administrative burden on the
Justice Department to secure already publicly available material for
a commercial publication is certainly not the commonly perceived
purpose of the FOIA,'' the appeals court said. ``Nonethleless, we
cannot find in the words of the statute any exemption to cover such
a situation.''
   The appeals court added that ``the burden in this case may not be
so excessive as the department makes out.''
   Government lawyers estimate that complying with the Tax Analysts
requests on a regular basis would cost nearly $75,000 a year for
search time alone.
   They say commercial publishers and law firms would flood the
Justice Department with similar FOIA requests if the appeals court
ruling is not overturned.
   The appeals court ruled that federal trial court opinions and
orders sent to the Justice Department are ``agency records'' under
the FOIA, and that they are being ``improperly withheld'' from the
tax magazine.
   In the appeal acted on today, government lawyers said the appeals
court was wrong on both points.
   The case is Justice Department vs. Tax Analysts, 88-782.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r a PM-RainierScare     01-09 0436</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Rainier Scare,0447</SECOND>
<HEAD>Seven Skiers Survive Storm, Avalanche on Mount Rainier</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By KATIA BLACKBURN</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>SEATTLE (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Seven skiers spent a night in a makeshift snow
shelter and then survived an avalanche after getting lost during a
sudden storm on Mount Rainier.
   ``It was an extremely calm and well-organized group, which really
made a big difference,'' Lonnie Rosenwald, 33, of Seattle. Their
ordeal ended safely Sunday when the group members met up with a
rescue team on their way down the mountain.
   Still, she said, ``There were lots of times we thought our lives
were in danger. There were times we didn't think we were going to
get out of there.''
   The skiers, all experienced at wilderness survival, left Saturday
on what was supposed to be a one-day back-country ski trip to the
Muir snowfield on the south flank of the 14,410-foot mountain. While
they were attempting to return that afternoon, a storm closed in and
they got lost in a steep valley, Rosenwald said.
   The skiers, on a trip sponsored by the Seattle Mountaineers Club,
tried until midnight to climb out of the valley and then decided to
build a structure and a small fire to survive the night.
   ``It was a wonderful structure,'' said Rosenwald. ``It was a
six-foot by three-foot hole in the snow, with a roof consisting of
skis, poles, and pine boughs covered by snow. It was rectangular. It
snowed all night and we stayed dry.''
   The group members, ranging in age from 31 to 68, was experienced
and fit and had proper clothing, Rosenwald said. No one panicked,
and no one got dangerously cold.
   On Sunday morning, as the skiers set out to return, five of them
were caught in an avalanche, Rosenwald said. Everyone was able to
``swim'' through the 200-foot slide safely except for one man, who
hit a tree and was slightly injured.
   They then opted to ski down the creek valley, correctly guessing
that it would take them to a road that leads to a National Park
Service facility at Paradise.
   Just before dusk, the group met a Rescue team that had been
searching for them in heavy snow and winds since daybreak Sunday.
   ``It was nice to see the rescuers, but we would have made it,''
Rosenwald said. ``There were 12 of them. They had food and water and
dry clothing. ... The park service brought us to Paradise, and let
us warm up.''
   Park ranger Bill Dengler said the skiers were in ``good condition
and delighted to get back inside and get into a warm area, out of
the cold.''
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r w PM-Scotus-ChildAbuse     01-09 0593</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Scotus-Child Abuse,590</SECOND>
<HEAD>Mother Who Refused to Disclose Whereabouts of Daughter Loses Appeal</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By JAMES H. RUBIN</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>WASHINGTON (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   A mother jailed since August 1987 for refusing
to disclose the whereabouts of her daughter, whom she says was
sexually abused by the child's father, lost a Supreme Court appeal
today.
   The justices, without comment, let stand a contempt citation
against Dr. Elizabeth Morgan, a District of Columbia plastic surgeon.
   Morgan, 41, was imprisoned in August 1981 for defying an order by
District of Columbia Judge Herbert B. Dixon Jr. that she allow her
daughter, Hilary, then 5, to spend a two-week unsupervised visit
with Dr. Eric A. Foretich.
   Foretich is Hilary's father and Morgan's former husband.
   Morgan sent the child into hiding to protect her, she said, from
sexual abuse by Foretich, who has denied abusing his daughter.
   The judge last Dec. 15 refused to free Morgan after more than 15
months in jail, saying he is not convinced her imprisonment will
fail to coerce her into disclosing Hilary's whereabouts.
   ``The coercion has only just begun,'' Dixon said. He added, ``It
could be a month, it could be a year, it could be more than that''
before he is convinced ``there is no realistic possibility of her
relenting.''
   Morgan has been held in civil contempt, imposed to coerce someone
into complying with a court order. The citation requires
imprisonment for an indefinite period. Criminal contempt, by
contrast, is imposed for a limited sentence as punishment.
   In her Supreme Court appeal, Morgan said her rights were violated
because Dixon conducted much of the hearing that led to the contempt
citation in private.
   The judge said he was trying to protect Hilary from public
disclosure of the details of her father's alleged sexual abuse. Many
of the specific charges were made public in a separate federal court
suit against Foretich by Morgan.
   Dixon ruled in 1986 that Morgan failed to prove Foretich had
abused Hilary. He said there was equal evidence on both sides of the
case. The judge permitted brief supervised visits for the father,
and in August 1987 granted Foretich a two-week unsupervised visit
that led Morgan to send her daughter into hiding.
   Morgan said Dixon was biased against her, and wrongly refused to
believe evidence indicating Hilary had been abused.
   An appeals court here ruled last August that Morgan's rights were
not violated.
   ``A review of the record shows that there was proabable evidence
on both sides of the issue of abuse,'' the appeals court said. It
added that it is unable to conclude that Dixon was ``clearly
erroneous'' in granting Foretich a two-week visit.
   The appeals court noted that a court-appointed guardian for
Hilary opposed the visit but had not argued that Dixon abused his
discretion in ordering the visit.
   The appeals court also upheld Dixon's decision to bar the public
from hearing Morgan's testimony about specific alleged acts of
sexual abuse.
   Morgan said the public has been prevented from hearing crucial
evidence that is vital to an issue of national debate.
   Her lawyers said studies show that sex abuse charges are made in
some 2 percent of contested custody and visitation cases.
   Calling the figure one of ``alarming frequency,'' they said
doubts have been raised about the ability of judges to referee such
disputes.
   Growing numbers of ``desperate parents have ... taken their
children and disappeared'' because the parents ``have lost faith in
the willingness and ability of the judicial system to protect their
children,'' Morgan's appeal said.
   The case is Morgan vs. Foretich, 88-737.

<SECOND>PM-BRF--Stormie Jones,0152</SECOND>
<HEAD>Heart-Liver Recipient Back For Tests</HEAD>
<DATELINE>PITTSBURGH (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The world's first recipient of a heart-liver
transplant is back in Children's Hospital of Pittsburgh for tests on
her liver.
   Stormie Jones, 11, was admitted around midnight Sunday, said
hospital spokeswoman Lynn McMahon. Her mother, Lois Purcell,
accompanied her from their home in White Settlement, Texas.
   Stormie looks well and is in good condition, but recent blood
tests found elevated levels of body toxins that the liver should be
removing, said Miss McMahon.
   Doctors Thomas Starzl and Henry Bahnson performed Stormie's
pioneering transplant operation at Children's Hospital in February
1984 when she was endangered by hyper-cholesterolemia, a disorder in
which great amounts of fat accumulate in the blood.
   She returned last October for reconstruction of a bile duct.
   Miss McMahon said it was not clear whether her new symptoms are
related to the bile duct problem.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r w PM-Scotus-PowerRates     01-09 0527</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Scotus-Power Rates,520</SECOND>
<HEAD>Justices to Consider New Orleans Electric Service Case</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By JAMES H. RUBIN</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>WASHINGTON (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Supreme Court today agreed to decide
whether federal courts should referee some disputes over electric
power rates between public utilities and local or state regulatory
agencies.
   The justices said they will hear an appeal by New Orleans Public
Service Inc., a utility company seeking to pass on to its customers
$135 million in costs.
   The high court, in a related ruling last June, severely limited
the authority of states to lower electric rates that public
utilities charge as a result of agreements with federal regulators.
   But that decision left unanswered whether federal judges should
rule in such disputes or leave these matters to state courts.
   The case acted on today stems from a nearly decade-old dispute
between New Orleans Public Service, or NOPSI, and the city council,
which under Louisiana law sets utility rates charged consumers in
New Orleans.
   The council barred NOPSI from charging customers for the costs
the utility incurred in buying electric power generated by the $3.6
billion Grand Gulf 1 nuclear power plant in Port Gibson, Miss.
   The rates charged NOPSI to purchase power from Grand Gulf were
set by the Federal Energy Regulatory Commission.
   Therefore, NOPSI argued, city and state officials are powerless
to prevent it from passing on the costs to its customers.
   The federal commission in 1985 ordered NOPSI to pay 17 percent of
the cost of constructing Grand Gulf 1.
   NOPSI, in a partial settlement with the city council in 1986,
agreed to absorb $51.2 million of the costs of Grand Gulf.
   NOPSI said the city gave assurances it would be allowed to
recover the remaining $135 million share of its costs through higher
retail rates over a period of time.
   But the council said the utility acted imprudently in failing to
resell the power it agreed to purchase from Grand Gulf and blocked a
pass-through.
   NOPSI sued in federal court for permission to pass on the $135
million to its customers, arguing that the federal commission's role
in the matter preempted the city council's power.
   A federal judge denied NOPSI's claim, and the 5th U.S. Circuit
Court of Appeals upheld the ruling last July.
   The appeals court said, ``A federal court with jurisdiction over
a matter that also lies within the jurisdiction of a state court or
agency should abstain from exercising its jurisdiction. New Orleans
Public Service must seek relief elsewhere.''
   The Reagan administration urged the Supreme Court to overrule the
5th Circuit court and order the federal courts to decide the dispute.
   The Justice Department said that at the very least it should be
made clear that federal courts have power to allow utilities to pass
on such costs temporarily to customers while the case is pending in
the courts.
   ``A delay in passing those costs through in retail rates will
often have consequences that are irreparable,'' government lawyers
said. ``It may create immediate cash flow problems and uncertainties
that may threaten bankruptcy, impede securities offerings and force
compromise settlements.''
   The case is New Orleans Public Service vs. New Orleans City
Council, 88-348.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>u w PM-Budget-Bush 1stLd-Writethru a0447 01-09 0955</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Budget-Bush, 1st Ld-Writethru, a0447,900</SECOND>
<HEAD>Bush Agrees With Intent Of Reagan Budget</HEAD>
<HEAD>EDs: Top 7th grafs new with reaction from Bush advisers; picks up
2nd graf pvs, bgng: Outgoing Budget</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By TOM RAUM</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>WASHINGTON (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   George Bush today studied President Reagan's
$1.15 trillion budget, and a transition official said the
president-elect ``agrees with its intent'' although still planned to
submit his own modifications.
   Bush transition spokeswoman Sheila Tate said that the vice
president himself might issue a brief statement on it later in the
day.
   But another transition official, who spoke on condition of
anonymity, said that Bush was prepared to say that Reagan's spending
outline ``says for one thing that you can meet the
Gramm-Rudman-Hollings targets without raising taxes.''
   It was a reference to the federal spending law that limits the
deficit in the fiscal 1990 year _ the one the new budget covers _ to
no more than $100 billion. The Reagan document envisions a $92.5
billion deficit.
   ``It's a good budget that does that,'' the Bush transition
official continued. ``Naturally, he agrees with its intent. And,
naturally, he'll have his own recommendations, or put forth
potential amendments, once he takes office.''
   ``But he won't be presenting any of those recommendations until
he's prepared to present all of them to the Congress, whatever time
that might be,'' the official said.
   The Reagan budget was designed, in part, to smooth the way for
the upcoming Bush budget plan, Reagan administration and Bush
transition officials said.
   Outgoing Budget Director Joseph R. Wright Jr. said Sunday the
final Reagan budget was designed with a ``dual purpose'' in mind,
summing up Reagan's philosophy while providing Bush ``with a good
base to begin negotiations with Congress.''
   Bush himself has said he won't submit a completely new plan of
his own, but will outline what he'll ``add here and take off
there,'' much of it to be spelled out in an address to a joint
session of Congress in early February.
   Congressional Democratic leaders have indicated they would like
to get Bush's final budget plan by no later than Feb. 20 so Congress
can begin work on its own budget and meet the goal of finishing work
on spending bills before the August recess.
   And leaders are indicating they are giving Bush the benefit of
the doubt on his much-touted concept of a ``flexible freeze'' to
hold overall spending increases generally to the rate of inflation _
but without a tax increase.
   ``My own view is that is very difficult,'' Senate Majority Leader
George Mitchell, D-Maine, said Sunday on NBC-TV's ``Meet the Press.''
   But, he continued: ``(Bush) has said it can be done and that he
will do it. ... My feeling is let's give him a chance to see if he
meant what he said and if he can do what he promised.''
   Senate Republican Leader Bob Dole of Kansas, appearing on the
same program, said he believes Bush is committed to his campaign
pledge not to raise taxes. ``I think George Bush meant it and if
people don't read lips in this town, I think they are going to be
left out somewhere,'' Dole said.
   Reagan's final gift to Bush is a fiscal 1990 budget document that
appears to meet that mathematical goal. Reagan's plan calls for
spending increases of 4.5 percent _ comparable to the 1988 rate of
inflation of about 4.4 percent.
   Under the Bush ``flexible freeze,'' any increased spending must
be offset in general by reductions in other areas.
   And, while Bush is certain to come up with his own specifics to
bring about some of the ``kinder, gentler nation'' programs he
talked about in his campaign, Reagan's budget appears to open the
way for such changes.
   Rather than calling for wholesale elimination of agencies and
programs, as past Reagan budgets have done, the new plan was
expected to be more selective, singling out specific programs and
spending items instead.
   That will make it easier for Bush to shape his own budget outline
by offering amendments to the Reagan plan.
   And some of Bush's pet campaign themes, including more funds for
education, were already contained in the Reagan proposal.
   ``There's no question, his (Bush's) first test of leadership will
be on this issue,'' Rep. Leon E. Panetta, D-Calif., chairman of the
House Budget Committee, said.
   ``The real question will not be the news in the Reagan budget,
but how the new president responds to the challenges contained in
the Reagan budget,'' Panetta said in an interview.
   Bush and his lieutenants have maintained a low profile in the
preparation of the Reagan budget. However, his choice for budget
director, Richard G. Darman, has made frequent trips to Capitol Hill
to meet with Republican budget writers.
   In the meetings, Darman indicated the forthcoming Bush proposals
would be general, and that the incoming president would put greater
emphasis on negotiations with Congress, according to participants.
   Bush has said he would send his budget negotiating team, headed
by Darman, to meet with congressional leaders at the outset of his
presidency.
   Darman was kept closely informed on details of the preparation of
the final Reagan budget. And Treasury Secretary Nicholas Brady _ a
close Bush confidant whom the vice president has announced he will
retain _ also played a role in shaping it.
   Wright, the outgoing Reagan budget director, said in an interview
on Sunday that the Reagan budget ``satisfies two requirements very
well.''
   ``It is President Reagan's last budget,'' he said. ``It is a
responsible, conservative budget. But it has also been put through a
reasonable test. If President-elect Bush wants to use it that way,
it provides him with a good base to begin negotiations with Congress
during a very tough year.''
</TEXT>

<FIRST>u w BC-BudgetText 6thadd   01-09 0417</FIRST>
<SECOND>BC-Budget Text, 6th add,420</SECOND>
<HEAD>WASHN: next century.</HEAD>
<HEAD>Other Priorities</HEAD>
<TEXT>
   Other areas of federal responsibility receive priority funding in
this budget:
   _To continue the Federal Aviation Administration's multiyear
program to increase its controller and inspector workforces and to
modernize the nation's aid traffic control systems, the budget
provides $7.7 billion _ a 17 percent increase over the 1989 level.
   _To alleviate the problems facing our savings institutions, I
propose that the Federal Savings and Loan Insurance Corporation
(FSLIC) spend $16 billion in 1989 and $9 billion in 1990 to address
the most serious thrift institution problems.
   The secretary of the treasury is developing a comprehensive plan
to resolve the savings industry's problems, and reform the financial
institution regulatory structure and deposit insurance system to
prevent a reoccurrence of these problems.
   I expect the secretary to submit his proposals to the new
president shortly.
   _To improve coordination of federal rural development programs
and to redirect funding toward needy rural areas and program
recipients, I am continuing support of the rural development
initiative coordinated by the secretary of agriculture.
   _By emphasizing housing vouchers, I would provide housing
assistance to 132,000 additional low-income households in 1990, 5
percent more than the 126,124 additional households receiving
housing subsidies in 1989.
   Housing vouchers can serve more low-income households at a lower
federal cost and provide greater opportunity for these families to
rent housing of their own choosing.
   _To maintain the progress we have been making in fostering state
and local education reform, I would sustain the present level of
spending on discretionary education programs at $18.5 billion, but
refocus those funds to put more money where the needs of the
disadvantaged and students with handicaps are greatest.
   _To continue the significant progress we have made in cleaning up
the environment, I recommend a $153 million increase for the
Environmental Protection Agency's regulatory, research and
enforcement programs.
   I also recommend an increase of $315 million for the Superfund
hazardous waste cleanup program in order to maintain the program's
momentum and support a stronger enforcement role.
   _Because changes in the Earth's natural systems can have
tremendous economic and social effects, global climate change is
becoming a critical concern.
   Our ability to understand and predict these changes is currently
limited, and a better understanding is essential for developing
policies.
   The budget proposes a coordinated and effective federal research
program on global change.
   This budget is accompanied by a report by the Committee on Earth
Sciences that describes this program and its strategy.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r w PM-Scotus-Bendectin     01-09 0436</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Scotus-Bendectin,440</SECOND>
<HEAD>Court Refuses to Revive Bendectin Lawsuits</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By JAMES H. RUBIN</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>WASHINGTON (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Supreme Court today refused to revive
lawsuits seeking millions of dollars in damages in behalf of
children with birth defects allegedly caused by the anti-nausea drug
Bendectin.
   The court, without comment, rejected an appeal by more than 800
families who sued Merrell Dow Pharmaceuticals, the maker of
Bendectin, in federal court in Ohio.
   Merrell Dow had agreed in a 1984 settlement to create a $120
million fund to compensate children with birth defects alledly
caused by their mothers' use during pregnancy of Bendectin.
   Under the terms of the settlement, the company did not admit the
drug had caused the defects.
   The settlement later was rejected by a federal appeals court,
which reinstated the suits against Merrell Dow. A federal jury
decided after a 22-day trial in 1985 that Bendectin did not cause
birth defects when taken in prescribed doses.
   The case was tried in Ohio because Merrell Dow has its
headquarters in Hamilton County there.
   The families that sued said they were denied a fair trial in part
because the presiding judge barred children under 10 and all
children with visible deformities from attending the trial. The
judge said allowing the young children and deformed children to
attend could inflame the jury and prejudice the case against Merrell
Dow.
   Those children were permitted to watch the trial on
closed-circuit television elsewhere in the court building.
   Also, the trial judge limited the trial's focus to determining
whether the families could prove that Bendectin causes birth defects.
   Only if the families were successful on that issue _ and they
were not _ would they then have had the opportunity at subsequent
trials to show that Merrell Dow was legally responsible for the
defects and to establish how much money the company must pay.
   The families said splitting up the issues in this manner unfairly
prevented them from presenting evidence that Merrell Dow was
negligent, guilty of fraud, failed to warn mothers of the drug's
dangers and violated a federal law on branding of drugs.
   The 6th U.S. Circuit Court of Appeals ruled last August that the
trial judge did not act improperly in imposing the restrictions.
   The appeals court rejected arguments that splitting up the trial
and barring young children and those with deformities from attending
created a ``sterile atmosphere'' that was unfair to the plaintiffs.
   ``Sterility is not necessarily the inevitable
consequence...merely because the jury may not hear the full evidence
of defendant's alleged wrongdoing,'' the appeals court said.
   The case is Hoffman vs. Merrell Dow, 88-704.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r w PM-Scotus-GrandJurors     01-09 0443</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Scotus-Grand Jurors,430</SECOND>
<HEAD>Court Lets Stand Missouri Decision on Grand Jury Members</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By RICHARD CARELLI</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>WASHINGTON (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Supreme Court today turned away arguments
that judges violate free-speech rights when keeping secret the names
of people selected as grand jury members.
   The justices, without comment, let stand a Missouri judge's
decision to bar public inspection of the names of grand jurors in a
double-murder investigation.
   St. Charles County Judge Lester W. Duggan convened a grand jury
last June to investigate James H. Williams Sr. and his wife, JoAnn
Williams.
   The couple was accused in the murder of Walter Scott, Mrs.
Williams' first husband.
   Williams also was accused of killing his ex-wife, Sharon Williams.
   After a reporter for the St. Louis Post-Dispatch asked for the
names of the grand jury members, Duggan ordered the names be kept
confidential ``in the interest of the safety and welfare'' of each
grand juror.
   The Pulitzer Publishing Co., which owns the newspaper, sought to
force Duggan to release the names but a state appeals court voted,
2-1, to uphold his orders.
   The Missouri Supreme Court turned down the newspaper's appeal
last Sept. 13.
   In the appeal acted on today, lawyers for the Post-Dispatch
argued that ``access to the names of those serving on the grand jury
should be presumed open.''
   They said any judge should be required to explain the special
circumstances existing before the presumption of openness could be
overriden.
   A practice of keeping the names of grand jury members secret
``may lead to the erosion of public confidence in both state and
federal grand juries,'' the appeal said.
   Past Supreme Court rulings, relying on the ``historical
openness'' of court proceedings and the free-speech guarantees of
the Constitution's First Amendment, have recognized the public's
right to attend criminal trials, including jury selection and
pretrial hearings.
   The newspaper's appeal said those decisions should be extended to
include access to grand jurors' names.
   But St. Charles County prosecutors urged the justices to reject
the Post-Dispatch's appeal.
   They drew a distinction between open trials and traditionally
closed grand jury proceedings, saying the latter ``are not subject
to the same rigid procedural safeguards.''
   The previously most recent grand jury in St. Charles County had
been convened in 1978, and names of grand jurors were made public
then.
   But county prosecutors said the 1978 grand jury was investigating
the purchase and renovation of a building, not ``a highly
publicized, gruesome double homicide.''
   ``Public policy dictates that jurors investigating a double
homicide would reasonably require a greater level of protection ...
from possible reprisals or pressure,'' the prosecutors said.
   The case is Pulitzer Publishing Co. vs. Duggan, 88-770.
</TEXT>

<HEAD>Theater In Philadelphia, Miss., Won't Show Movie</HEAD>
<HEAD>Eds: INSERTS 1 graf after 7th graf pvs, `The movie...', to add background.
Picks up 8th graf, `Other theaters...'.</HEAD>
<DATELINE>PHILADELPHIA, Miss. (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The owner of the town's only theater
vowed not to show the film ``Mississippi Burning,'' a fictionalized
account of the killings of three civil rights workers here 25 years
ago.
   ``We're not going to show it. No way. It's not worth it,'' Lula
Ellis, who owns the Ellis theater, said Sunday. ``I just see no
reason why we should play it.''
   Ellis had said that church groups were threatening to boycott the
theater if it showed the movie, directed by Alan Parker and starring
Gene Hackman and Willem Dafoe.
   On June 21, 1964, civil rights workers James Earl Chaney, 21, who
was black, and whites Andrew Goodman, 20, and Michael Schwerner, 24,
disappeared after they were released from jail in Philadelphia. They
had been stopped on speeding charges after they had been
investigating the burning of a church by the Ku Klux Klan.
   The three were found 44 days later, shot to death and buried in a
soggy earthen dam in Neshoba County.
   Ellis, who earlier this week said she hadn't made up her mind
about showing the film, said Sunday she reached a decision against
the movie after after discussing it with her son Shelly Steiger, the
theater manager.
   The movie has refocused national attention on this town of 6,800
people, which is 70 miles northwest of Jackson. The film has been
released in five cities and is scheduled for nationwide release
Friday.
   During most movies at Ellis' cinema, blacks customarily sit in
the balcony, with whites, a few blacks and Choctaw Indians sit down
in the orchestra seats. Steiger said this was by custom and was not
required by the theater management.
   Other theaters in Mississippi will run the movie.
   Monte Royal, owner of two cinemas in Meridian, which is about 40
miles southeast of Philadelphia, said he will feature the film by
Feb. 10.
   ``Even though the picture is fiction based on a factual incident,
it is indeed pertinent and part of our past,'' Royal said.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r w PM-Scotus-Lattimore     01-09 0410</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Scotus-Lattimore,420</SECOND>
<HEAD>Court Refuses to Grant Pittsburgh Professor Access to Grand Jury
Records</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By JAMES H. RUBIN</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>WASHINGTON (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Supreme Court today refused to grant a
college professor access to sealed grand jury records in the case of
Owen J. Lattimore, a China scholar accused in the 1950s of being a
communist sympathizer.
   The court, without comment, rejected an appeal by Prof. Robert P.
Newman of the University of Pittsburgh, who is writing a Lattimore
biography.
   Lattimore, 88, and now retired in Pawtucket, R.I., was accused by
the late Sen. Joseph R. McCarthy, R-Wis., of helping to ``lose
China'' to the communists.
   A professor at Johns Hopkins University in the 1950s and an
expert in Asian affairs, Lattimore was indicted on perjury charges
by a federal grand jury after the Justice Department accused him of
lying to a congressional committee.
   Before that committee, he had denied ever being a communist agent
or sympathizer.
   Parts of the government's case were declared unconstitutional in
1952, and again in 1955 a judge threw out another indictment. The
government then dropped Lattimore's prosecution.
   Public Citizen, an organization representing Newman, said the
grand jury records could provide valuable insights into the McCarthy
era.
   ``The Lattimore indictments call into question the extent to
which unchecked prosecutorial power and congressional influence may
have been used to engineer a political prosecution and to employ the
grand jury for improper purposes,'' lawyers for Public Citizen said.
   Newman said the public has an important interest in being
informed about what happened in the grand jury probe.
   But the U.S. Circuit Court of Appeals here ruled last April that
the rule for preserving grand jury secrecy ``makes no provision for
disclosure of transcripts to a private party for historical research
purposes.''
   Newman and Public Citizen said the ruling ``places critical
information about historically important chapters of history off
limits to historians and the public.''
   They said other courts have allowed public disclosure of grand
jury testimony when it is in the public interest even if federal
rules do not authorize specifically opening the files to help
historians.
   The Reagan administration urged the Supreme Court to reject
Newman's appeal.
   ``Even if dressed up as a significant project of historical
scholarship, requests of (this) type...could not easily be
distinguished from journalistic inquiries into subjects of public
interest or requests based simply on individual or public
curiosity,'' the Justice Department said.
   The case is Newman vs. U.S., 88-548.
</TEXT>

<HEAD>WASHN: its strategy.</HEAD>
<TEXT>
   _Last year's fires on federal forestlands indicated the need for
more timely funding for annual firefighting costs.
   I therefore propose that two new federal wild land firefighting
accounts be established in the departments of agriculture and
interior.
   _To further strengthen our energy security, I propose legislation
authorizing the sale of the naval petroleum reserves to the private
sector in exchange for cash and oil to be added to the strategic
petroleum reserve.
   I also propose the establishment of a separate 10 million barrel
defense petroleum inventory.
   _To provide for the timely completion of my Reform '88 management
improvement program I propose an additional $103 million for 1990,
to further improve our management and credit systems.
</TEXT>
<HEAD>MAJOR PROGRAMS ARE REFORMED TO ACHIEVE DEFICIT REDUCTION</HEAD>
<TEXT>
   The program structure and incentives underlying many domestic
federal programs need to be altered to promote greater efficiency
and cost-effectiveness.
   _Current farm price support programs are far too costly.
   For the period 1986-89 an estimated $130 billion in federal
spending for farm-related assistance programs provided an average of
nearly $600,000 per farmer.
   Much of this assistance goes to farmers with high incomes _ more
than twice the U.S. family average.
   I therefore propose outlay reductions for the price and income
support programs of $2 billion in 1990 and additional annual
reductions of between $2 and $2.5 billion in each year from 1991
through 1994.
   In addition, I urge reform of the counterproductive sugar price
support program.
   _The rapidly rising costs of the Medicare program need to be
moderated.
   I propose a reasonable increase in the Medicare prospective
payment system rate and reductions in hospital capital payments and
special graduate medical education payments.
   Also, in an effort to restrain excessive growth in supplementary
medical insurance (SMI) costs, I propose extension of the current
law SMI premiums, limitations on physican payments, reductions in
payments for certain overpriced procedures, and reforms in the
durable medical equipment payment system.
   Medicare spending would still grow by 9 percent between 1989 and
1990 under these proposals _ but not by the 13 percent that would
occur under current law.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r w PM-Scotus-Water     01-09 0352</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Scotus-Water,350</SECOND>
<HEAD>Court Acts in Ohio Water-Rate Case</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By JAMES H. RUBIN</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>WASHINGTON (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Supreme Court today refused to bar
city-owned water systems from charging some non-resident customers
higher rates than other non-residents even if there is no
cost-related reason.
   The court, without comment, turned down an appeal by Summit
County, Ohio, officials who said they are being charged unfair rates
by Akron, Ohio.
   The case began in 1981 when Akron officials sued Summit County
for allegedly breaking a 1979 contract between the two governmental
bodies. The city said the county illegally wittheld payment for
water supplied by the city.
   The county countersued, claiming it entered into the contract
under duress and was being charged unreasonable and discriminatory
rates by Akron.
   The county said it was being charged rates some 90 percent higher
than another Akron water customer in the area, the neighboring city
of Tallmadge.
   Summit County said it was forced to pay more because it has no
alternative source of water.
   The Ohio Supreme Court upheld the higher rates for Summit County,
ruling county officials signed a contract with the city they must
honor.
   ``The degree of control which the courts will exert over such
(city-owned) public utilities is strictly limited to protecting
residents of the municipality from the imposition of rates which are
unreasonable or which discriminate among such residents,'' the state
court said.
   The Ohio court said it presumed that the contract signed by the
county was made ``through the normal give and take of contractual
negotiation.'' The county ``entered the bargaining process freely
and without duress.''
   The state court added there is nothing wrong in charging the
county higher rates based on its need for the water, even if Akron's
cost of supplying it is no greater than in providing water for
Tallmadge.
   ``Where rates for water from a municipally owned public utility
are set forth in a contract, such rates will not be struck down as
discriminatory even where no factor exists which could justify the
rate discrimination,'' the state court said.
   The case is Summit County vs. Akron, 88-596.
</TEXT>
</DOC>
<DOC>

<HEAD>Budget Calls For Education Programs To Be Scrapped; Spending Up Slightly</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By JILL LAWRENCE</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>AP Education Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>WASHINGTON (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Reagan administration today proposed
eliminating two dozen federal education programs while slightly
increasing overall education outlays from $20.6 billion this year to
$21.2 billion in fiscal 1990.
   Some $541 million saved by the cuts, plus another $212 million
from cost savings officials hope to achieve from the guaranteed
student loan program, would go to expand existing programs for
disadvantaged students from kindergarten to graduate school.
   The proposed budget does not incorporate any of the education
spending ideas set forth during President-elect Bush's campaign.
They included a $500 million merit schools program for schools
serving disadvantaged children and a $50 million infusion for a
magnet schools program designed to encourage parental choice.
   But Bush, who pledged during the campaign to be ``the education
president,'' is expected to rework the budget in the weeks following
his Jan. 20 inauguration. The education community asserted last week
that he needs to request an extra $2.5 billion for fiscal 1990 in
order to begin to fulfill his pledge.
   Education Secretary Lauro Cavazos said last month that ``I did
the best I could'' to secure the best possible budget for his
department.
   The document does not contain specific new initiatives reflecting
his own priorities _ cutting illiteracy and the dropout rate _ but
that could come later. Cavazos has said that after Bush takes over,
``We will be getting into some rather specific kinds of programs.''
   Among the programs on the chopping block under the Reagan plan
are the Women's Educational Equity Act research program, which
officials complain has outlived its purpose, and a two-year dropout
prevention demonstration project due to expire.
   Also suggested for extinction are programs the administration
considers duplicative, and programs officials say should be financed
by the private sector. The latter include impact aid to school
districts serving areas with tax-exempt federal installations; Star
Schools, which would put satellite technology in schools; and a
number of scholarship and fellowship programs.
   The largest dollar increase under the Reagan plan _ and one of
the only boosts higher than the anticipated 3.4 percent inflation
rate _ would go to Pell grants, the chief federal assistance program
for low-income post-secondary students.
   Current budget authority for the program is nearly $4.5 billion.
That would rise by $256 million under the Reagan plan _ a 5.7
percent hike. The maximum grant amount of $2,300 would stay the same
and the number of students receiving aid would rise 46,000 to 3.1
million.
   Reagan would increase outlays for Chapter 1, the major federal
program for low-achieving children in poor neighborhoods, from the
current $4.2 billion to $4.3 billion in fiscal 1990. Most of the
extra money would be for grants funneled to areas with the highest
concentrations of needy students.
   The Reagan budget would hike spending authority for vocational
and bilingual education programs by about 3.4 percent. Spending
authority for vocational education would rise to $949 million while
bilingual would increase to $157 million. Outlays are anticipated to
be slightly less.
   The administration is hoping to save $212 million on the
guaranteed student loan program by reducing special allowance
payments used as an incentive to lenders to participate, and by
reducing guaranteed reinsurance to lenders and state guarantor
agencies from 100 percent to 90 percent on the dollar. Congress
would have to approve both moves.
   The entitlement program currently costs the government about $3.2
billion in interest and default payments. Officials expect loan
volume to rise from $11.5 billion this year to $12.5 billion in
fiscal 1990, adding another 278,000 students to the 4 million-plus
already participating in the program.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r a PM-ChurchClosings 1stLd-Writethru a0545 01-09 0570</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Church Closings, 1st Ld - Writethru, a0545,0578</SECOND>
<HEAD>Archdiocese Closing Plan Leaves Some Parishes Bitter, Others Elated</HEAD>
<BYLINE>Eds: SUBS 11th graf, `The announcement...'. to give detail on number
of black Catholics in archdiocese. Picks up 12th graf pvs, ``On Sunday...''
^By GRETEL WIKLE</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>DETROIT (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Archdiocese of Detroit softened a proposal to
close or merge 48 city parishes, but Cardinal Edmund Szoka said more
than a quarter of the city's 112 parishes still would close by June
30.
   Szoka announced Sunday that 30 mostly inner-city churches would
be closed, while an additional 25 parishes would be given a year to
prove their viability. In September, a task force had recommended
that 43 churches be closed and five others merged into parishes with
more members and greater resources.
   Even with the reduced number of parishes involved, there is no
precedent in the U.S. Roman Catholic church for such large-scale
closings, Msgr. John Tracy Ellis, a historian at the Catholic
University of America in Washington, said today.
   ``The situation was entirely different in the past,'' he said.
``It was a question of how many new parishes to open, not to close.
Insofar as I can recall, there is nothing like this.''
   The church closings were necessary, officials said, because of a
white flight from the city that reduced Detroit's Catholic
population from 100,000 housholds in 1976 to fewer than 50,000 today.
   ``The church, especially the church in the city of Detroit, finds
itself in much different circumstances in 1989 than those that
existed in '69, or '59, or '29, or 1909,'' Szoka said. ``It cannot
act as though nothing has changed.''
   Szoka, who had defended the earlier church closing plan, said
Sunday the 25 churches whose survival is in doubt would be asked to
increase membership, services and parish staff.
   ``All we are ask is that they try. ... I mention to them that in
no way is there a Damocles (sword) hanging over their head,'' he
said. ``It is meant rather to be a stimulus.''
   The cardinal called for more blacks to enter religious life in
his announcement Sunday and said he also intended the closings to
help end a ``plantation mentality,'' with white parish leaders
running churches attended largely by blacks.
   One of the 30 closing churches is expected to become a black
Catholic historical museum, he said.
   The announcement in September resulted in a wave of protest
against the 1.5 million-member archdiocese, which takes in six
counties in the Detroit area. There are an estimated 50,000 to
60,000 black Catholics in the archdiocese, church officials said.
   On Sunday, at Our Lady of the Rosary, one of many churches that
appealed the closing plan, parishioners were elated at the one-year
reprieve.
   ``There's a year and a pronouncement by the archdiocese to work
with parishes to see some sort of improvement,'' said Patrick
Gossman, a member of Our Lady of the Rosary. ``We'll go on with a
certain sense of hope and work at it.''
   But there was bitterness at churches slated to close.
   At St. Philip Neri, sniffling could be heard among the 70 people
attending Mass as the Rev. Donald Walker announced that the east
side church would close.
   ``This wasn't supposed to happen,'' Walker said, referring to the
difficulty he had in making the announcement. ``I guess you just
resent it when someone comes in and tells you it's time to die.''
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r a PM-TrainSearch     01-09 0353</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Train Search,0362</SECOND>
<HEAD>Author, Volunteers Search for Buried Locomotive</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By NANCY BURKHART</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>BENNETT, Colo. (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Best-selling author Clive Cussler and some
300 volunteers found no signs during a weekend search of a
locomotive believed buried in a dry creek bed during an 1878 flood,
but he says they have not given up hope.
   ``In order to find it, we need to know where it isn't,'' Craig
Dirgo of Cussler's National Underwater and Marine Agency said Sunday
after the search ended. ``That's the key to any search.''
   Searchers braved 20-degree weather and gusty winds to sift
through a half-mile area near here for remnants of the
Kansas-Pacific locomotive.
   The engine, 19 cars and a caboose fell 30 feet from a railroad
bridge in 1878, when a flood struck the area 30 miles east of
Denver. Twelve of the cars and the locomotive were reported to have
been buried in the sand.
   Some researchers believe that the railroad subsequently salvaged
the expensive engine, but Cussler and his band of searchers are
still hoping it's still in the ground. The group hopes to squeeze in
one more weekend of hunting before spring to search a two-mile area
downstream from the original bridge.
   ``We know it's here and we will be back,'' Dirgo said.
   Volunteer Eric Walker of Darwin, Australia, found a part from a
railroad flat car. Others found slag and coal, which officials said
may have been used for ballast by the railroad.
   Cussler said if the locomotive is found, it will belong to the
landowner. Mike Pinzenscham, who watched the dig's progress with his
8-year-old son, Justin, said his family has owned the land for about
25 years.
   Through the Arvada-based agency, Cussler, the author of ``Raise
the Titanic,'' has located 54 sunken vessels including a dirigible.
But this is his first buried train hunt.
   The agency's next project will be a search for the steamship New
Orleans, which was built by Robert Fulton in 1811. Cussler said the
ship's bottom was torn out when it ran aground on a stump in the
Mississippi River at Baton Rouge, La.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>u w BC-BudgetText 8thadd   01-09 0403</FIRST>
<SECOND>BC-Budget Text, 8th add,400</SECOND>
<HEAD>WASHN: current law.</HEAD>
<TEXT>
   _I also propose reforms in the Medicaid program to reduce
spending growth between 1989 and 1990 to $1.7 billion, or 5 percent,
rather than the $3.3 billion, or 9 percent, that would occur under
current law.
   These reforms reinstate successful incentives employed in the
early 1980s.
   My budget also proposes restructuring federal financing of
administrative expenses to give states greater incentives to operate
their administrative systems as efficiently as possible.
   _The government often continues programs at the federal level
that are either duplicative or are no longer needed, or more
appropriately undertaken by other levels of government or the
private sector.
   This is the case with the Economic Development Administration,
Amtrak, urban mass transit discretionary grants, and most operating
subsidies for the Postal Service.
   Efforts to reverse this situation have been undertaken by prior
administrations as well as my own.
   These programs should be eliminated.
   The budget proposes termination of 82 programs that are not
needed to satisfy national priorities.
   _Under current law, outlays for federal employee retirement and
health benefits are estimated to grow from $51.3 billion in 1989 to
$55.9 billion in 1990.
   I propose freezing retirement cost-of-living allowances (COLAs)
and other reforms to hold the 1990 level to slightly above that for
1989, reducing the growth that would otherwise occur by $4.4
billion.
</TEXT>
<HEAD>ACHIEVING A PROPER FEDERAL ROLE AND IMPROVING MANAGEMENT</HEAD>
<TEXT>
   As the federal government grew, it took on improper
responsibilities, and managed its programs inefficiently.
   We undertook to return the federal government to its proper role.
   We also initiated a major program to improve the management of
the remaining programs.
   These priorities are continued and expanded in this budget.
  
</TEXT>
<NOTE>Privatization</NOTE>
<TEXT>
   _ The government and the private sector should each do what is
does best.
   The federal government should not be involved in providing goods
and services where private enterprise can do the job cheaper and
better.
   Accordingly, my budget proposes that a number of federal
enterprises be opened to the private sector, through public
offerings or outright sales.
   Following our success in the sale of Conrail and the sale of $21
billion in selected loan portfolios, I am proposing sale of the
naval petroleum reserves, the Alaska Power Administration, and the
Southeastern Power Administration.
   I also propose sale of the federal government's helium-processing
assets, excess real property, and a further $4.3 billion in loan
portfolios.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r i PM-China-Africa-Unrest     01-09 0466</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-China-Africa-Unrest,0489</SECOND>
<HEAD>Ghana Embassy Calls for China to Release Student</HEAD>
<HEAD>With PM-China-Africans</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By KATHY WILHELM</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>BEIJING (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Ghana's top diplomat today called on China to free
an African student arrested in a racial brawl, while other African
diplomats urged their students to end a class boycott begun by the
fighting.
   ``We are not satisfied. The case has not been handled properly,''
said Ghana's charge d'affaires, Kwadwo Kwakye. ``We want (him) to be
released.''
   Alex Dosoo of Ghana, a student at Hehai University in eastern
Nanking city, was arrested Dec. 31 and charged with beating and
seriously injuring a Hehai employee during the Dec. 24 clash.
   Police gave two other African students from Gambia and Benin 15
days ``disciplinary detention'' as punishment for having ``tried to
stir up trouble.''
   In a development apparently unrelated to the racial tensions, an
Ugandan medical student jumped from a sixth-floor dormitory window
at a campus in southern Canton city Friday, killing himself.
   Xinhua, the official news agency, said Nsubuga David Mugwanya,
26, had been depressed about financial problems and examination
failures.
   The clash between African students and Chinese school workers and
students began when the Africans brought Chinese women to a dance
Dec. 24.
   Kwakye said the fight ``happened on a campus and should have been
handled by the university and not handed over to the police.''
   In a telephone interview, he said he did not think Dosoo should
face criminal charges.
   He said authorities took three days to inform the embassy of
Dosoo's arrest and still have not advised them of any formal charges
or what punishment the student might face.
   ``We have protested his detention and the fact that we were not
notified,'' said Kwakye.
   He was among 11 African diplomats who met Chinese authorities and
African students in Nanking over the weekend.
   Kwakye said he told Ghanaian students today that ``it was within
their rights'' to boycott, ``but that they must be careful not to
contravene the law.''
   The official Xinhua News Agency quoted other diplomats as saying
they plan to advise their students to return to classes and obey the
law. The approximately 140 African students in Nanking have not
attended classes since the clash.
   About 500 African students at Beijing Languages Institute have
boycotted classes since Dec. 4 to protest charges by Chinese
students that an African man assaulted a Chinese woman in her
dormitory. The Africans say the story is fabricated and demand an
apology.
   Institute president Liu Bisong sent a letter today urging
boycotting students to return to class. Liu said Chinese students at
the school were wrong to protest last week, but that the class
boycott also was ``erroneous.''
   Ghana is the only African country that has said it is considering
bringing its 26 students in China home.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>u a PM-POWCallBack     01-09 0350</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-POW Call Back,0359</SECOND>
<HEAD>POW, Rescuer Reunited After 44 Years</HEAD>
<DATELINE>NEW YORK (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   An American who escaped a massacre at the hands
of the Japanese in World War II was reunited today with one of his
Filipino rescuers, 44 years after being freed.
   ``It was a great feeling,'' said Glenn McDole of Des Moines,
Iowa, describing his reaction to seeing Mary Anne Ancheta, now of
Algoma, Wis.
   In a brief appearance on ABC's ``Good Morning America'' show,
Mrs. Ancheta said the reunion fulfilled a longtime dream.
   ``All along I have been hoping that I would meet one of them,''
she said.
   In 1944, the malaria-weakened McDole and several other escapees
were brought to a Filipino guerrilla camp run by Maziro Major, Mrs.
Ancheta's father. Major, now 88, is retired after an Army career and
lives in Manila.
   At the time, Mrs. Ancheta was 7 years old. She said she
remembered McDole because he was the one ``shaking like a leaf''
from the effects of the disease, but she hadn't remembered his name.
   Last month, Mrs. Ancheta, now a nurse, saw an article about a
reunion of the prisoners of war. The story quoted McDole, and she
called him on New Year's Eve.
   That first attempt at a reunion ran afoul when the tape on
McDole's answering machine ran out before she could give her last
name and telephone number. But she tried again after reading an
article about McDole's anguish over missing the call from ``Mary
Anne from Wisconsin.'' The two chatted for the first time last week.
   McDole, 67, now chief of the patrol division in the Polk County
sheriff's office, said he was one of about 150 American prisoners
being held at Palawan Prison when American troops landed nearby.
   He said the captors ordered the prisoners into long trenches,
then threw gasoline and torches on them. About 20 prisoners broke
through the side of a trench and scrambled down a cliff to safety,
but many were shot before they could run away.
   McDole said he was treated and sheltered for about a month by
Major's group.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>u w PM-Budget-HealthandSocial     01-09 0498</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Budget-Health and Social,460</SECOND>
<HEAD>Reagan Would Spend More Than $1 Billion on AIDES</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By DEBORAH MESCE</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>WASHINGTON (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   President Reagan today asked Congress to
increase spending on AIDS research by 24 percent and trim the growth
of the huge Medicare and Medicaid health programs by cutting
payments to states and fees to hospitals and physicians.
   Overall, the proposed fiscal 1990 budget for the Department of
Health and Human Services would increase 5.7 percent to $424.4
billion in fiscal 1990.
   The department is the largest Cabinet agency in terms of
spending, accounting for 37 percent of federal outlays, including an
anticipated $257 billion on Social Security for the next fiscal year
beginning Oct. 1.
   The budget proposal would add $311 million to the fight against
the spread of the deadly acquired immune deficiency syndrome, ``our
highest public health priority.'' The increase would raise spending
for research, education and prevention of the disease to $1.6
billion.
   The department estimates another $925 million will be spent on
treatment and income support to AIDS victims through Medicaid,
Medicare, Disability Insurance and other service programs.
   The overwhelming bulk of the department's spending is earmarked
for entitlements _ Social Security, Medicare and Medicaid _ over
which the agency has little budget control since eligibility for the
programs is set by Congress.
   If no changes were made, Medicare _ which pays for the health
care of about 34 million elderly and disabled Americans _ would grow
by 14 percent in fiscal 1990.
   However, Reagan proposes trimming that growth rate to 10 percent
by cutting $3.5 billion from fees paid to physicians and hospitals.
His Medicare budget would total $94.9 billion.
   About a third of the Medicare cut, $1 billion, would come from
reducing the additional disbursements that teach hospitals receive
to cover their costs of training interns and residents.
   Other reductions would be made in payments to hospitals for
capital costs and fees to physicians for certain procedures,
including radiology, anesthesiology and surgery, under the Medicare
payments system.
   A new part of Medicare is the Catastrophic Health Insurance
program, which is intended to be entirely self-supporting. It is
financed by premiums paid by Medicare recipients, which are
projected to total about $8.6 billion in fiscal 1990. But the
program is projected to spend only $4 billion that year.
   Medicaid _ the program that provides health care for about 26
million low-income Americans _ would grow about 9 percent next year
if unchanged. The administration, however, is proposing to slice the
growth to 5 percent growth or by about $1.4 billion to a spending a
total of $36 billion.
   Much of the reduction would come from reducing payments to the
states, which administer the program under federal guidelines, by
about $1.1 billion.
   Benefits under the Social Security program are protected and
recipients. Based on administration estimates of what inflation will
be this year, the budget projects Social Security recipients getting
a 3.6 percent cost-of-living increase in their benefits in January
1990.
</TEXT>
