<DOCNO> AP890109-0124 </DOCNO>
<FILEID>AP-NR-01-09-89 1251EST</FILEID>
<FIRST>r a AM-PollMethod     01-09 0462</FIRST>
<SECOND>AM-Poll Method,0475</SECOND>
<HEAD>Method Used in Media General-Associated Press Poll on Homelessness</HEAD>
<HEAD>With AM-Poll-Homeless Bjt</HEAD>
<DATELINE>NEW YORK (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   This Media General-Associated Press poll is based
on telephone interviews Nov. 10-20 with 1,084 adults in the
continental United States. The phone numbers were selected randomly
by computer and interviews were conducted with the adult who had the
most recent birthday.
   The results were weighted to adjust for variations in the sample.
The poll has a three-point margin of error, meaning that if every
adult American were asked the same questions the results
theoretically would be within three points of the poll's results, 19
times out of 20. The error margin is larger for subgroups _ four
points for women, for example, and five for Democrats.
   There are other sources of potential error in polls, including
the wording and order of questions. These are the questions and
responses in this poll; because of rounding, sums may not total 100:
   1. In your opinion, how serious a problem is homelessness in the
United States? Is it very serious, fairly serious, not too serious,
or not serious at all? Very: 68 percent. Fairly: 24. Not too: 5. Not
at all: 1. Don't Know or No Answer: 2.
   2. Do you think the problem of homelessness is getting worse,
improving, or staying about the same? Worse: 68. Improving: 3. Same:
24. DK-NA: 5.
   3. Who do you think should be primarily responsible for helping
homeless people _ the federal government, state government, local
government or private charities? Federal: 32. State: 24. Local: 18.
Charity: 9. DK-NA: 17.
   4. Do you think federal spending on helping the homeless should
be increased, decreased or remain the same? Increased: 60.
Decreased: 6. Same: 27. DK-NA: 7.
   a. (If increased) Would you be willing to pay more taxes for
that? Yes: 86. No: 11. DK-NA: 3.
   5. Thinking about why most people are homeless, would you say
society is mainly at fault, or the homeless personally are mainly at
fault? Society: 45. Homeless: 33. DK-NA: 22.
   6. Do you think homelessness is a problem only in big cities, or
in communities of all sizes? Cities: 24. All sizes: 74. DK-NA: 2.
   7. Does the federal government do enough to prevent people from
becoming homeless, or not? Yes: 27. No: 58. DK-NA: 15.
   8. Should the federal government build more subsidized housing
for poor people? Yes: 65. No: 27. DK-NA: 8.
   9. How about people who are homeless _ should the federal
government spend more to provide them with housing? Yes: 62. No: 27.
DK-NA: 10.
   10. Should people be allowed to live on the streets if they want
to? Yes: 45. No: 51. DK-NA: 4.
   11. Are there homeless people in your community? Yes: 59. No: 37.
DK-NA: 4.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r a PM-SeditionTrial 2ndLd-Writethru a0573 01-09 0787</FIRST>
<SECOND>PM-Sedition Trial, 2nd Ld-Writethru, a0573,0803</SECOND>
<HEAD>Trial Finally Gets Under Way, Nearly One Year And $3 Million Later</HEAD>
<HEAD>Eds: SUBS 5th graf to UPDATE with jury selection completed. Pick
up 6th graf pvs: `Up to ...'.</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By CAROLYN LUMSDEN</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>SPRINGFIELD, Mass. (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   A trial of avowed revolutionaries that
has already cost the government millions of dollars moved toward
opening arguments almost a year after lawyers began selecting jurors.
   The three remaining defendants of eight who were captured in one
of the largest manhunts in U.S. history are accused of sedition, or
conspiring to topple the government by force, under a rarely invoked
law whose meaning has been the subject of much courtroom wrangling.
   ``It is a serious threat to fundamental liberties,'' said Peter
Avenia, one of six defense lawyers.
   Prosecutors contend, however, that the charge of sedition fits
the group they describe as a dangerous band of terrorists. A Boston
courthouse worker lost a leg and 21 other people were wounded in one
of the score of bombings the group allegedly plotted from 1976 to
1985.
   Jury selection began last March with a vast pool of jurors.
Selection of the final few jurors in the U.S. District Court trial
wound up this morning with the seating of 12 jurors and five
alternates. Opening arguments were slated to begin Tuesday.
   Up to 350 witnesses could be called. A closed-circuit camera has
been installed with monitors in another room in case defendants are
ejected for unruliness. Observers must pass through two metal
detectors to reach the courtroom, which an estimated 40 marshals
take turns guarding.
   The defendants each face up to 60 years in prison and $60,000 in
fines if convicted on the charges of seditious conspiracy,
racketeering conspiracy and racketeering enterprise.
   Their attorneys argue that the trial is a waste of money because
their clients already have been convicted on bombing and bank
robbery charges and all but one are serving prison sentences. The
cost so far is estimated at well over $3 million.
   The defense attorneys say the seditious conspiracy statute is so
broad that it could be used against protesters blocking streets.
   ``If people engage in political dissent and think this can't
happen to them, they should read the statute,'' said Avenia.
   The statute makes it a crime for ``two or more persons in any
state or territory to conspire to overthrow or put down or destroy
by force the government of the United States.''
   The government has used the statute only a few times since it was
first invoked to punish Confederates during the Civil War, said
Arthur D. Wolf, a professor at Western New England College in
Springfield.
   It was used to keep protesters from hindering the draft during
World War I and to prosecute Puerto Rican nationalists accused in an
assassination attempt against then-President Harry Truman. Last
April, nine white supremacists were acquitted of seditious
conspiracy charges in Arkansas.
   In the case going to trial here, an FBI hunt for the defendants
swept through hundreds of towns in Massachusetts, New Hampshire,
Vermont, Connecticut, New York and Ohio in 1983.
   Arrested in their Cleveland house on Nov. 4, 1984, were Jaan
Laaman, 40; his wife, Barbara J. Curzi-Laaman, 31; and Richard
Williams, 41. Agents in Deerfield, Ohio, arrested Raymond Luc
Levasseur, 42, and his wife, Patricia Gros Levasseur, 34, the same
day. Carol A. Manning, 33, and her husband, Thomas Manning, 42, were
apprehended six months later in Norfolk, Va. Christopher King, 38,
was arrested in North Attleboro in 1982.
   The federal trial was moved to Springfield in 1987 so that
defendants could be housed together at a jail in nearby Hartford,
Conn.
   The only defendants, however, are Williams and the Levasseurs.
The five others either have changed their pleas to guilty or been
dropped from the case. Mrs. Levasseur is out on bail, having
finished a five-year term for possessing false identification and
harboring her fugitive husband.
   King was tried separately in 1987. He pleaded guilty that
December to one count of seditious conspiracy and was sentenced to
seven years in prison.
   In August, the government dropped charges against Manning and
Laaman, saying Manning already was serving 53 years and Laaman, 45
years, for bombings. Manning also got a life sentence for the 1981
shooting death of a New Jersey state trooper.
   Mrs. Manning pleaded guilty two weeks ago to all three counts.
She was sentenced to five years, to be served concurrently with her
15-year term on bombing charges.
   On Friday, the First Circuit Court of Appeals upheld a decision
suppressing evidence against Mrs. Curzi-Laaman because it was seized
without a search warrant. The decision meant she would be tried
separately. She is serving a 15-year sentence.
</TEXT>

<HEAD>Report Police Discover `Terrorist' Weapons</HEAD>
<DATELINE>BELGRADE, Yugoslavia (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Police in the central Yugoslav
republic of Bosnia have discovered a cache of firearms and
ammunition ``used by terrorists,'' media reports said Monday.
   Authorities in Sarajevo, the republic's capital, said the find
included a U.S.-made automatic rifle, four pistols, eight revolvers
and more than 1,000 rounds of ammunition, reports said.
   The newspaper, Borba, quoted police as saying the weapons and
ammunition were of the kind ``most often used by terrorists.''
   To date, officials have refused to identify a man detained before
the New Year's holidays on allegations of trying to smuggle the
rifle and four pistols into the country.
   A subsequent search of his home yielded the remaining arms, Borba
reported.
   The police statement did not identify the terrorist group to
which the detained man is accused of belonging. However, leaflets
and publications of banned Croatian nationalist groups were shown
with the state TV report on the arms cache.
   Croatian extremists have been blamed for several bombings and
attacks in Yugoslavia in the past 20 years.

<FIRST>r a AM-Brites     01-09 0547</FIRST>
<SECOND>AM-Brites,0566</SECOND>
<HEAD>Bright &amp; Brief</HEAD>
<DATELINE>LOUISVILLE, Ky. (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Television portrayals of lawyers as
``aggressive, loud, obnoxious persons who insult judges'' is
ungentle- manly and -womanly, and the Kentucky Bar Association wants
to be sure life doesn't imitate TV.
   The association has decreed that Kentucky's lawyers should be
courteous in the courtroom and friendly with their colleagues and
that the lawyers should never intentionally mislead or deceive their
adversaries.
   The Code of Professional Courtesy, endorsed by the association's
governing board in September, carries no penalties.
   But George Long II, the association's president, said it will
remind lawyers to watch their Ps and Qs.
   Long acknowledged that the law inherently involves conflict. But
he said it should be practiced courteously.
   Richard Frymire, head of the association's Committee on Lawyer
Professionalism, which drafted the code, said it is designed to
counter television portrayals of lawyers as ``aggressive, loud,
obnoxious persons who insult judges'' _ and a growing sense that is
how real lawyers are supposed to behave.
   ``I think it's terrible that a profession like the law has to
have this type of code imposed,'' said Martin Johnstone, the chief
Jefferson Circuit Court judge. ``But from what I've seen over the
past few years, I think it's necessary.''
   Still, some practitioners say the code may clash with more
important ethical requirements, and describe it as vague and even
silly.
   ``Why do we have to be friendly?'' asked Louisville lawyer C.
Thomas Hectus. ``Am I supposed to inquire as to how are the wife and
kids?''
   Writing in the association's magazine, Bench &amp; Bar, U.S. District
Judge William Bertelsman said lawyers have become ``more aggressive
and harder to get along with.'' He said judges must devote
increasing time to refereeing abusive tactics that range from
``benign incivility to outright obstruction.''
   ``I absolutely believe a lawyer can zealously represent clients
and abide by all precepts'' of the code, said Frymire.
</TEXT>
<DATELINE>SMITHFIELD, R.I. (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Take his land. Please.
   Robert Luther has 1.2 acres of land, zoned for development, that
he can't sell or even give away. In the meantime, he has to keep
paying taxes that were $1,275 last year.
   The Smithfield resident inherited the parcel in 1985 from his
brother. It is zoned for industry in an area where there has been
considerable recent development. But 80 percent is swampland, and
any alteration for construction requires federal and state consent.
   ``I've had a couple of people look at it, but when they have had
engineers look at it they decided to forget it,'' he said. Two
engineering firms hired by Luther have said the land is unsuitable
for construction in its present state.
   Luther then tried to give it to Providence College, his alma
mater, but they weren't interested.
   So he tried to give it to the town, but the town council isn't
welcoming the offer with open arms either.
   ``You don't look a gift horse in the mouth,'' said Town Council
President Anthony B. Simeone. But the council wants to study the
matter.
   Luther said some have suggested he just stop paying taxes, in
which case the town would seize it and try to recoup the lost taxes
at a tax sale. Luther said he'll wait to hear from the town council
before deciding his next move.
</TEXT>

<HEAD>Man Wakes To Car Overhead</HEAD>
<DATELINE>BALTIMORE (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Tyrone Nelson thought he heard an explosion
Monday morning and jumped from beneath the covers only to find a car
hanging over his bed.
   His wife, Darlene, 36, sustained leg injuries when she was hit by
the front end of the car that crashed into their first-floor bedroom
and by debris that filled the room. She was treated at the Greater
Baltimore Medical Center and released, police said. Nelson was
unhurt.
   Officer Greg Taylor said a Baltimore County Police officer was
following a suspected stolen car when the car went out of control,
moved along a 100-foot grass strip and then crashed into the
apartment.
   Just before the crash, Officer Richard Theis lost control of his
car as well and hit a fence, but was uninjured.
   In the confusion, the driver of the car that hit the apartment
escaped, although the transmission of the car landed in the Nelsons'
bathroom and the force of the impact left a gaping hole in a bedroom
wall, shattered windows and mirrors and disrupted electrical power.
   Police said the intruding car had been registered to a dealership.

<SECOND>AM-Legislative Movers,0202</SECOND>
<HEAD>Lawmakers Are Movers Before They Become Shakers</HEAD>
<DATELINE>MADISON, Wis. (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Legislature doesn't convene until later
this month, but members have already begun tackling one of the
toughest issues _ office space.
   It's a cutthroat political game, played like this: Legislative
leaders get the best offices, veteran legislators fight over the
leaders' old offices and new members scramble for whatever's left.
   Rep. Marlin Schneider of Wisconsin Rapids, who's in charge of
office assignments, humorously threatened to call in an air strike
by the Wisconsin National Guard unless warring factions in the
Capitol's north wing resolved their own territorial dispute.
   In the same spirit, he proposed capital punishment _ and
something worse than that _ for legislators who try to make off with
their old office furniture.
   ``Failure to cooperate may result in death by firing squad, or no
pork for you in the budget,'' he warned colleagues in a memorandum
on unauthorized furniture moving.
   But even that dire threat didn't keep Rep. Joseph Wineke of
Verona from moving his desk chair from his old office to his new one.
   ``I stole that chair personally years ago, and I'm keeping it,''
Wineke said.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r w AM-Nickles-ChemicalWeapons     01-09 0244</FIRST>
<SECOND>AM-Nickles-Chemical Weapons,220</SECOND>
<HEAD>Nickles: Deny Access To US Markets To Firms Involved In Chemical
Weapons</HEAD>
<DATELINE>WASHINGTON (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Sen. Don Nickles, R-Okla., said Monday he will
seek passage of legislation to deny foreign chemical companies
access to U.S. markets if they are found to have assisted in the
proliferation of chemical weapons.
   Nickles said the bill will be an effort ``to put some teeth'' in
a proposed international agreement banning the use, storage and
production of chemical weapons worldwide.
   ``This is an effort to make sure that if we do catch them we
would do something about it,'' said Nickles, noting that certain
West German chemical companies are alleged to have helped Iraq and
Libya attain chemical warfare capability.
   Nickles, who attended the ongoing conference on chemical weapons
in Paris, told a news conference that while he found the meeting
helpful, he continues to believe that ``the utopian goal of banning
nuclear weapons will be close to impossible.''
   He said that events like the use of poison gas in the Iran-Iraq
war show that even some signers of the 1925 international agreement
banning the use of chemical weapons have clearly violated it.
   ``Measures are lacking that would impose some penalties if
countries don't comply,'' he said. ``We have to have some teeth
behind these treaties.''
   He said he will seek passage, in partnership with Senate
Republican Leader Bob Dole of Kansas, of legislation imposing
sanctions on individual companies involved in chemical weapons
proliferation.
</TEXT>

<TEXT>

didn't return from work Saturday evening. But when she did not call
or come home overnight, Johnson went to the hospital Sunday morning.
He and a hospital administrator found Ms. Hinnant's body in a small,
fourth-floor office.
   Hinnant, a native of Columbia, S.C., was a 1982 graduate of the
Medical University of South Carolina and had worked in Washington
before coming to Bellevue in 1987, police said.

<FILEID>AP-NR-01-09-89 1336EST</FILEID>
<FIRST>u w BC-Budget-Excerpts     01-09 1263</FIRST>
<SECOND>BC-Budget-Excerpts,1200</SECOND>
<DATELINE>WASHINGTON (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Here are excerpts from the text of the budget
message which President Reagan sent to Congress on Monday.
</TEXT>
<TEXT>
   Eight years ago many in this country were concerned about the
future of our economy, our government and, indeed, the nation
itself. ...
   To resolve the economic problems then facing us, our
administration proposed a recovery program centering on four
fundamental principles:
   _Reduce personal and business tax rates.
   _Reduce the rate of growth of federal spending.
   _Reduce the federal regulatory burden by eliminating unnecessary
restrictions while protecting the public's interest and safety.
   _Support a moderate and steady monetary policy to bring inflation
under control.
   To rebuild our defense capabilities and restore America's
standing in the world we proposed expanded national security and
international programs. ...
   Today, the American people can be proud of the progress that has
been made on each of these fronts. ...
</TEXT>
<TEXT>
   The one area in which I have been persistently disappointed
throughout my term of office has been in the efforts to bring the
budget under control.
   Time and again I have proposed measures to help curb federal
domestic program spending. Time and again these proposals have been
rejected by Congress. ...
</TEXT>
<TEXT>
   The fiscal year 1990 budget, my last, represents a continuation
of my efforts to reduce the federal budget deficit through restraint
in domestic spending.
   The budget I am submitting today complies with the deficit
targets set in the Gramm-Rudman-Hollings (G-R-H) Act, by proposing
measures that meet the 1990 deficit target of $100 billion and
assure a steady reduction in the deficit leading to a balanced
budget in 1993. ...
</TEXT>
<TEXT>
   This budget shows that a gradual elimination of the deficit is
possible without raising taxes, without cutting into essential
social programs, without devastating defense, and without neglecting
other national priorities.
   It can be done in a reasonable, responsible way _ with discipline
and fairness.
   New taxes are not required. ...
   We have an opportunity this year to put the worst of the deficit
problem behind us and enable the next administration to begin its
term of office with a clean slate and with the promise of continuing
prosperity. ...
</TEXT>
<TEXT>
   To address urgent national priorities within the deficit limit
set by the G-R-H Act, my budget proposes that some programs _ such
as those for AIDS research and prevention, drug enforcement, and
technology development _ receive significant funding increases,
while others are reduced, reformed, or, in some cases, terminated.
...
</TEXT>
<TEXT>
   Maintaining peace and protecting our country are the foremost
responsibilities of the federal government.
   Defense budget authority declined in real (inflation-adjusted)
terms for the fourth straight year with funding of $299 billion for
1989.
   This trend cannot continue without severe impact on combat
readiness.
   Therefore, my budget requests defense funding of $315 billion in
budget authority and $303 billion in outlays for 1990, and $331
billion in budget authority and $314 billion in outlays in 1991. ...
</TEXT>
<TEXT>
   This budget reflects my belief that addressing the problem of
AIDS must remain a top priority:
   _Preventing and alleviating suffering from the Human
Immunodeficiency Virus (HIV), which causes AIDS, is our highest
public health priority. Federal support for research, prevention,
and treatment exceeds $2.1 billion in 1989, and will approach $2.8
billion in 1990. This budget asks for $1.6 billion, or 24 percent
over 1989, for Public Health Service HIV funding. ...
</TEXT>
<TEXT>
   The program structure and incentives underlying many domestic
federal programs need to be altered to promote greater efficiency
and cost-effectiveness.
   _Current farm price support programs are far too costly.
   For the period 1986-89 an estimated $130 billion in federal
spending for farm-related assistance programs provided an average of
nearly $600,000 per farmer.
   Much of this assistance goes to farmers with high incomes _ more
than twice the U.S. family average.
   I therefore propose outlay reductions for the price and income
support programs of $2 billion in 1990 and additional annual
reductions of between $2 and $2.5 billion in each year from 1991
through 1994. ...
   _The rapidly rising costs of the Medicare program need to be
moderated.
   I propose a reasonable increase in the Medicare prospective
payment system rate and reductions in hospital capital payments and
special graduate medical education payments.
   Also, in an effort to restrain excessive growth in supplementary
medical insurance (SMI) costs, I propose extension of the current
law SMI premiums, limitations on physican payments, reductions in
payments for certain overpriced procedures, and reforms in the
durable medical equipment payment system.
   Medicare spending would still grow by 9 percent between 1989 and
1990 under these proposals _ but not by the 13 percent that would
occur under current law. ...
</TEXT>
<TEXT>
   As the federal government grew, it took on improper
responsibilities, and managed its programs inefficiently.
   We undertook to return the federal government to its proper role.
   We also initiated a major program to improve the management of
the remaining programs.
   These priorities are continued and expanded in this budget. ...
</TEXT>
<TEXT>
   The persistence of the budget deficit is overwhelming evidence
that the federal budget process is fundamentally flawed.
   Past efforts at ``reform'' have been directed largely toward
protecting a large portion of domestic spending from real fiscal
discipline. ...
   Except for last year's on-time performance, Congress'
self-imposed budget deadlines have usually been missed, and massive
continuing resolutions and reconciliation bills have been the rule
rather than the exception.
   These large, cumbersome bills provide cozy hiding places for
hundreds of special interest add-ons, which line-item veto authority
would permit the president to challenge. ...
   _I remain committed to and urge approval of a constitutional
amendment requiring a balanced budget.
   The amendment should require a supermajority vote (at least 60
percent) in the Congress to increase taxes. ...
   _My successors should be given the authority, subject to
congressional override, to veto line-items in annual appropriations
bills, in authorizing legislation that provides or mandates funding
for programs, and in revenue bills.
   Such authority would permit the elimination of substantial waste
and would be an effective instrument for enforcing budget
discipline. ...
   _The annual budget process consumes too much time and energy.
   A biennial budget would reduce the repetitive budget tasks, allow
more time for considering key spending and revenue decisions,
provide less scope for gimmicks that give the illusion of
``savings,'' such as shifting spending from one year to another
without affecting the underlying programs, and permit the
realization of real savings that would be possible with a more
assured availability of funds. ...
</TEXT>
<TEXT>
   The accomplishments of the American people in the past eight
years will always be for me a source of pride.
   However, we must continue our recent progress in reducing the
federal deficit.
   Deficit reduction is a key national priority, written into law by
the G-R-H Act, which, despite its defects, legislated a process to
achieve a balanced budget.
   This budget achieves the 1990 target of the amended act, and
projects a budget balance in 1993.
   It preserves legitimate programs for the aged and needy, provides
for adequate national security, devotes more resources to other
high-priority activities, and accomplishes all this without raising
taxes.
   Tax increases are not needed.
   History shows that they would simply be used by the Congress to
increase spending.
   Tax increases have been overwhelmingly voted down in the last
three presidential elections.
   I call upon the Congress to enact this budget.
   Higher taxes are not needed _ as this budget demonstrates _ but
genuine deficit reduction through moderating the growth in spending
is essential to enable the next administration and Congress to
address the nation's agenda for the future.
</TEXT>
</DOC>

<DOCNO> AP890109-0135 </DOCNO>
<FILEID>AP-NR-01-09-89 1341EST</FILEID>
<FIRST>r w AM-Budget-Revenues     01-09 0619</FIRST>
<SECOND>AM-Budget-Revenues,610</SECOND>
<HEAD>Reagan Would Block Tax Cut for Fliers, Make State Workers Pay for
Medicare</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By JIM LUTHER</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>AP Tax Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>WASHINGTON (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   True to his word, President Reagan's final
budget calls for no general tax increase, although it would halt a
scheduled cut in aviation taxes and require all state and local
government workers to pay for Medicare.
   The budget that Reagan submitted to Congress on Monday includes a
$2.1 billion tax cut for corporations and $39 million of tax relief
for the oil and gas industries.
   Overall, revenues would drop by $1 billion, compared with current
law, under the budget for fiscal 1990, the 12 months that begin next
Oct. 1.
   Most of the revenue changes had been proposed a year ago; in
fact, the president has submitted some of them for several years
running, only to have them ignored or rejected by Congress.
   Reagan's written message cited his final budget as evidence
``that a gradual elimination of the deficit is possible without
raising taxes....''
   ``Tax increases are not needed,'' the president continued. ``Tax
increases have been overwhelmingly voted down in the last three
presidential elections.''
   However, among the hundreds of tables in the budget documents is
one showing that during Reagan's eight years in office, Congress
passed and he signed 13 separate tax increases that will cost
taxpayers $150 billion during the next budget year. Reagan-era tax
reductions will save taxpayers $377 billion next year.
   In addition to the tax changes he proposed for 1990, Reagan
recommended $1 billion of fees on those who use selected government
services. The largest single provision in this package, $394
million, would raise the 1 percent fee for VA loan guarantees to 3.8
percent and make it permanent.
   Another recommendation would require middle- and upper-income
veterans to pay a portion of the cost of government medical care for
non-service-connected problems.
   Commercial and recreational boaters would have to pay a $25
annual charge for using Coast Guard-patrolled waterways; entrance
fees would be imposed for Corps of Engineer recreational facilities;
charges at Forest Service facilities would be broadened, and the fee
on Commodity Credit Corp. loan guarantees would double, to 1.2
percent.
   Among the tax proposals:
   _Repeal a law requiring that taxes on airplane tickets (8
percent), air shippers (5 percent) and plane fuel be cut in half
next Jan. 1 unless federal spending for airports and airways reaches
a certain level. That level is not expected to be reached, but
Reagan called for keeping those taxes at current rates, which would
cost shippers and travelers $1.2 billion.
   _Require all state and local government workers to pay the tax
that funds Medicare; those hired before April 1, 1986, are not
paying, even though most are eligible for Medicare because of
earlier, non-government employment or through their spouses. This
would raise $2 billion.
   _Permit corporations that operate abroad to allocate at least
two-thirds of their total research costs to domestic income, saving
them $1.7 billion.
   _Make permanent a 20 percent tax credit for increased spending
for research, saving those businesses $400 milllion.
   _Liberalize deductions permitted oil and gas producers for
depletion, at a cost to the treasury of $39 million.
   _Increase Internal Revenue Service enforcement activities,
producing a $300 million boost in tax collections.
   Reagan again proposed to sell U.S. oilfields at Teapot Dome,
Wyo., and Elk Hills, Calif., for $1 billion, and all or parts of
several power-marketing administrations, including those in Alaska
and the Southeast, which would bring in $1.3 billion in 1990.
   The budget also proposes to raise money by selling or allowing
prepayment of $6.9 billion of government loans for $5.8 billion,
including those for college housing, and Small Business
Administration loans for business and disaster relief.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r i AM-China-Dissidents     01-09 0703</FIRST>
<SECOND>AM-China-Dissidents,0729</SECOND>
<HEAD>Dissident Tells Govt To Free Prisoner; Another Activist Released</HEAD>
<BYLINE>By JOHN POMFRET</BYLINE>
<BYLINE>Associated Press Writer</BYLINE>
<DATELINE>BEIJING (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   China's most prominent dissident on Monday called
for the release of the nation's most famous political prisoner. In
Shanghai, meanwhile, a pro-democracy activist was let out of jail.
   Fang Lizhi, an internationally respected astrophysicist, said
freeing all political prisoners, especially Wei Jingsheng, ``would
be a humanitarian gesture and would have a beneficial effect on our
social morale.''
   Fang made his call in a letter to Chinese leader Deng Xiaoping
that was obtained Monday.
   Wei, an electrician by profession, wrote a series of articles on
the need for democracy in 1978 during the ``Beijing Spring,'' a
period of relative political liberalization shortly after the end of
the ultra-leftist Cultural Revolution. He was arrested on March 29,
1979, and sentenced to 15 years in prison for
``counterrevolutionary'' crimes.
   Amnesty Internatiomal, a London-based human rights group, has
adopted Wei as a prisoner of conscience. Human rights groups have
variously reported that Wei has died or is suffering from severe
schizophrenia in custody.
   Amnesty International estimates thousands of Chinese are in
prison for crimes of conscience.
   Fang was expelled from the Communist Party and fired from his
post as vice chairman of the Chinese Science and Technology
University in Hefei in 1987 for allegedly fomenting pro-democracy
unrest among the nation's students.
   He later was given a position as a high-level researcher at the
Chinese Academy of Sciences and allowed to travel abroad. China has
denied Fang's most recent request to go to the United States.
   In winter 1986-87, hundreds of thousands of students marched in
cities and called for democracy in China.
   Yang Wei, a former student at the University of Arizona, was one
of the demonstrators.
   On Monday, Yang was released from the Ti Lan Qiao Prison in
Shanghai after serving one year of a two-year sentence for
``counterrevolutionary'' activities.
   The Shanghai Foreign Affairs office said Yang was released Monday
because he had already spent about a year in custody before being
sentenced.
   In a telephone interview with The Associated Press, Yang said his
first concern was the health of his father, who has cancer.
   ``I'm going to see him today,'' said Yang. ``He is in the
hospital and he is in serious condition.''
   Yang said he did not regret marching for democracy and writing
for ``China Spring,'' the banned periodical of the Chinese Alliance
for Democracy, a U.S.-based dissident group. But he said he was
unclear whether he would continue working for political change in
China.
   Yang described his treatment in prison as ``pretty good.''
   ``It seemed that I was given special treatment,'' he said.
   Yang, 33, who received a masters degree in molecular biology from
Arizona, said he would like to return to the United States to
continue his studies.
   Sources close to the family said Yang's release was delayed by
several hours because three members of the Chinese Alliance for
Democracy were apprehended in Shanghai over the weekend with copies
of ``China Spring'' and a letter for Ywng.
   The alliance was set up in 1983 by a former Chinese citizen in
order to promote democracy in China. Chinese officials have declared
it an illegal organization.
   The men _ a Chinese-American, a Chinese-Canadian and a Chinese
citizen _ were put on flights out of China after they admitted
breaking the law, official reports said.
   It was not clear why the Chinese citizen, who is a student at
Utah State University, was allowed to leave.
   Yang was sentenced in December 1987. He had been held without
charges since January 1987 in the student protests.
   Prosecutors accused Yang of stirring up anti-government protests
in Shanghai. The court said he put up ``reactionary slogans'' at
Shanghai's Fudan University on Dec. 22, 1986, during student
demonstrations.
   On Dec. 3, 1987, the U.S. Congress called for Yang's release,
saying he had broken no Chinese or U.S. laws.
   China labeled the move ``an undisguised interference in China's
internal affairs.''
   Yang's wife, Che Shaoli, a medical student in the United States,
said she was thrilled at his release. ``But I am sad, too,'' she
said in a telephone interview from New York. ``I don't know when I
will see him.''
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r w AM-US-Mexico     01-09 0190</FIRST>
<SECOND>AM-US-Mexico,170</SECOND>
<HEAD>US Says New Mexican Official Under US Indictment</HEAD>
<DATELINE>WASHINGTON (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The State Department said Monday a top Mexican
official newly appointed by President Carlos Salinas de Gortari is
under indictment for alleged involvement in a car theft ring in
southern California.
   ``We believe that persons under indictment should face their
charges in court,'' department spokesman Charles Redman said.
   Redman did not name the official but published reports over the
weekend identified him as Miguel Nazar Haro, the chief of a newly
created intelligence unit in the Mexico City police department.
   The car theft ring was smashed in 1981 by the FBI and California
police.
   The Washington Post said in its Saturday editions that several
top antinarcotics and law enforcement officials appointed to
Salinas' six-week old government are suspected of having links to
major Mexican drug traffickers. The Post cited Mexican and foreign
sources.
   In response, Redman said, ``I would note ... that the Mexican
government does conduct a substantial anti-narcotics effort and
President Salinas has announced plans for new programs which, in his
words, are `to make life miserable,' as he has said, for the
traffickers.''
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r i AM-Poland-Strike     01-09 0155</FIRST>
<SECOND>AM-Poland-Strike,0160</SECOND>
<HEAD>Miners Interrupt Work in Dispute Over Working</HEAD>
<DATELINE>WARSAW, Poland (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   About 1,500 workers at a coal mine near
Jastrzebie in southern Poland staged a successful two-hour work
stoppage Monday seeking improved working conditions, the banned
Solidarity trade union said.
   Miners at the Morcinek mine wanted a six-hour limit to the amount
of time miners can be forced to work in high temperatures, said
Wojciech Maziarski, a spokesman for the Solidarity Information
Service in Warsaw.
   They also asked for bonus pay for working under such conditions,
he said.
   Maziarski said management agreed to the demands after the
``warning strike,'' which lasted from 6 a.m. to 8 a.m., and several
hours of talks with workers' representatives from a Solidarity
committee at the mine and the official OPZZ trade union.
   There was no immediate information about the stoppage in the
official media, and a dispatcher at the mine refused comment when
reached by telephone.
</TEXT>

<FIRST>r i AM-Soviet-Inventions     01-09 0304</FIRST>
<SECOND>AM-Soviet-Inventions,0311</SECOND>
<HEAD>New Law to Give Tax Breaks for Inventions</HEAD>
<DATELINE>MOSCOW (AP) </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Soviet Union is trying to foster a
technological spurt with a proposal to offer tax breaks for
state-run enterprises that put new inventions to use, an official
said Monday.
   I.S. Nayashkov, chairman of the State Committee on Inventions and
Discoveries, told a news conference that a draft law on inventions
proposes giving a three-year income tax exemption to state-run
businesses that put inovations to use.
   Officials hope such a move will ``increase the interest of
enterprises in inventive work or in research and development,'' he
said. President Mikhail S. Gorbachev says more modern technology is
a key to modernizing Soviet industry and providing a higher standard
of living.
   The draft law was published in the official press Dec. 27, and
must be approved by the Supreme Soviet, or parliament, at one of its
sessions in 1989. It is unclear yet when it will go into effect.
   The law also would allow the establishment of venture banks to
encourage investors and set aside ``patent funds'' to help inventors
without the means to support their work, Nayashkov said.
   He said there has been ``little movement and weak dynamics'' in
recent years, with the number of applications for patents and the
number actually registered remaining virtually without change.
   Since 1983-84, Soviets have applied for between 170,000 and
190,000 patents annually and 70,000-80,000 have been granted, he
said. The remainder were judged not to offer anything substantially
new or valuable.
   Nayashkov said the law also seeks to better protect foreigners by
bringing Soviet patent law closer to a convention signed in
Washington in 1970, and gives Soviets the right to register their
inventions in foreign countries instead of the Soviet Union as long
as their inventions don't concern defense matters or state secrets.
